###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Receiving e-mails from group

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-03-08T12:05:51+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<apskqgF6gibU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<dba8222d-409b-4d45-9548-82180be8e27c@googlegroups.com> <a97ac30d-e0c6-4969-a8e3-36d1aa9b7f51@googlegroups.com> <kh2if8$lu4$1@reader1.panix.com> <066ee4b4-2693-4798-93be-a3ce07164dc1@googlegroups.com> <kh7rfp$hoc$1@reader1.panix.com> <c3746c05-6c18-4388-a36d-dc3993b2181b@googlegroups.com>`

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Wednesday, 6 March 2013 16:39:21 UTC, docd...@panix.com wrote:
>> The effort is appreciated. DD
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> probably would have been too limited in function. Thanks to all.

Here's a thought:

Create a clean Virtual Machine image with Windows XP or whatever your 
favourite OS is.

(You download the Virtual Box software from Oracle, then create a new 
machine using your Windows install disk. Save the clean machine image as a 
snapshot; it is a mouse click.)

Download the full Edition of Net Express, which has a free trial for 30 
days, to the Virtual Machine.

Put your COBOL development  Project sources on a different machine, or 
organise them into a single directory on the VM so you can back it up.

Poddle away with NetExpress for 30 days.

After 30 days, backup your project sources to a different machine or 
external device.

Restore the clean machine from the snapshot.

Install NetExpress.

Restore your project sources.

Good to go for another 30 days...

Now, I'm not advocating this for people who are seriously developing in 
COBOL; you should bite the bullet and buy the compiler.  But for people who 
just want to dabble, or for hobby development, it is not a bad solution and 
you aren't limited to 2000 lines etc.

I have need of MF COBOL (NetExpress) VERY occasionally when I need to check 
out how something works in that environment. I do not develop solutions in 
that environment. (The fact that I would have to license the runtime is a 
showstopper for me and also the support for COM is not good enough to meet 
my requirements. (You cannot batch compile COM projects in NetExpress and 
every component you build HAS to have an NE Project so the methods can be 
registered.) The whole business of developing COM in NE, although viable, is 
ugly and cumbersome compared to Fujitsu where it is easy and simple. Having 
said that, the implementation of COBOL as a language is excellent in NE just 
as it is in NetCOBOL, but NetCOBOL has no runtime fee.)

I use the above solution if I need Micro Focus NetExpress for anything. 
(Like I said, that is VERY rarely and it is around a year since I last 
loaded the Micro Focus VM  It is a bit tedious to have to re-install and 
reload the sources but I do it so rarely it is acceptable. The full install 
takes areound 30 minutes. For a hobbyist, the inconvenience once every 30 
days is probably an acceptable alternative to buying the software.

My advice to people who want to play with COBOL would be as follows:

1. Use Open COBOL. (If it supported OO, I'd be using it exclusively...It's 
an excellent product.)

2. Learn C#. (It's free and you're a "programmer", right?) There are some 
really superb free videos to get you started and that was how I learned it. 
There may be little commercial advantage in learning C# (or any "desktop" 
programming language apart from scripting) as the world moves on to mashups 
and pre-packaged functionality, but you can do more with it and you learn a 
lot of other stuff along the way which means that your programming becomes 
richer and you can take on more challenging tasks. You can also get stuff 
working quicker because the tools are better.

I guess if you want to learn something with commercial advantage, CSS3, 
HTML5, Javascript, VBscript, and general Web Technology is the way to go.

I'm currently in the process of completely refurbishing the PRIMA and 
COBOL21 web sites.

(http://primacomputing.co.nz and http://primacomputing.co.nz/cobol21 )

The sites will be combined and the content is being completely overhauled. 
If you haven't had a look around these sites, now would be the time...

They will be replaced some time in the next few weeks.

There is free stuff and there is information about getting procedural COBOL 
into objects. The COBOL21 site is really aimed at COBOL guys and has some 
fun stuff and free components.
 I don't want to lose this but it will be heavily revamped in the new 
incarnation. The most visited parts will be preserved (in a new format) but 
some of it will go. There will be some new portals and pages also, so it 
should still be a COBOL-friendly place to go.

Between them the 2 sites have logged over 30,000 unique visitors since they 
were put up, so I'm satisfied my effort is not being wasted.

The new development is occupying all of my time and is a huge undertaking. 
This comes back to tools. Dreamweaver and VS 2012 are being used, and I'm 
also playing with a new tool from Microsoft called WebMatrix. It is a free 
download and it is very useful if you want to get a toe in the water of web 
development.

SQL Server 2012 (64 bit) has also been placed on the Web Development machine 
which is running Windows 8. I'm pleased to report no problems with any of 
these products and the SQL Server Management Studio 2012 accesses my local 
and remote DBs (which were built with SQL Server 2005 and 2008 on 32 bit 
platforms) with no problems at all.

The new site will have a new style that is aimed at surface devices and 
looks like Windows 8.  It will reflect the current move away from desktop to 
surface technology and it won't look a lot like most current web sites. In 
my own tests with what I have so far, I'm pretty happy with it, although 
HTML5 video needs some work. I have videos at the moment which use HTML5 if 
your Browser supports it, and falls back to Flash if it doesn't. Although 
the sound and definition in HTML5 are better than Flash, it doesn't use the 
whole screen (even when it is told to) and that's something I am looking 
into.

The new site is designed to make maximum use of the desktop real estate so 
things are easier to read and the underlying technology is ASP.NET and 
CSS3/HTML5. I am using IE 10 for the development although it will be tested 
with Firefox and Chrome.

The point is that if you are used to poddling around with COBOL, you might 
find some web development satisfying... The tools are free and the 
information is available for free also. There are videos and tutorials to 
get you started. If anyone downloads WebMatrix and has a crack at some web 
development, please let us know your thoughts.

Pete.
```

```
problem of getting mail centralized, so I can access it from any machine on

Web Mail and I have configured Outlook 2007 with the necessary Mail accounts 
I need, to receive from all of the various mail addresses I use.)

little free formatter called "OEQuoteFix". This is like an outlook plugin 
and it seems ot work very well on XP and Win 7 (haven't tried it yet on Win 
8).

somewhere central. This is easy for the LAN but it's more problematic if I 
am travelling. I've looked at using Skydrive, or a similar service but my 
typical "current" mail file is over 2 GB and that makes it hard for the XP 
machines to access.

I archive ALL mail that is sent or received by me and I can retrieve emails 
that go back 20 years...:-) (This paranoid behaviour started when I got fed 
up with being misquoted and realized that computers are very good at 
organizing and filing things...I offline my mail file and restart it about 
once a year.)

As soon as I get a chance (far too busy at the moment) I'll sit down with 
the Win 8 machines and look at a proper solution; meanwhile, I'm using ONE 
XP machine to hadle mail and newsgroups....

Pete.
```

<a id="t-5c143343f0"></a>
## Thread: Remember EDP? — 10 msgs

### Remember EDP?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-03-09T11:04:17+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<apvmlnFr50rU1@mid.individual.net>`

```

http://www.webpronews.com/microsoft-thinks-the-future-of-information-technology-lies-in-intelligent-technology-2013-03

A long time ago, when the World was all new and sparkly, commercial computer 
systems were used for "data processing". Those of us who worked with them 
used the generic term "EDP" (Electronic Data Processing) to describe what we 
did.

Then technology moved along and the spehere of electronic influence expanded 
to include networks and even telepone systems. A new breed of programmer 
came along and they said they were involved in "IT" (Information 
Technology).

Just as we managed to get our heads around the implications of that, those 
pesky technologists started producing devices where the software and 
hardware were blurred and computing devices no longer knew their place. A 
revolution in networking and communications brought computing to the masses; 
the ways in which people interact with machines became diverse and subtle. 
The days when Man told Computer exactly what to do and the relationship was 
very clear, started to disappear as the Man/Machine relationship underwent a 
paradigm shift.

Now, they are telling us we will interact with an environment rather than a 
device. And we will do it using what comes naturally to us (gesture, voice, 
movement...) The GUI will be replaced by the NUI (Natural User Interface).

This is the advent of "Intelligent Technology" and it is redefining not just 
the "IT" letters, but the whole way the world views computers.

It made me smile to see "Big Data" being recognised as available and 
processable, having spent most of my life working in places where they do 
exactly that.

It's a bit like watching your grandchildren suddenly become aware of what 
you have known all your life.

However, they are bringing fresh new ways of looking at and dealing with it 
and we can bet that the Electronic Data Processing in Corporations in 10 
years time, will be a far cry from how we did it back in the latter half of 
the last century.

So now I work in "Intelligent Technology"...

I must get my busness cards reprinted... :-)

Pete.
```

## Thread: Universities are failing us with COBOL? — 32 msgs

### Universities are failing us with COBOL?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-03-12T22:55:46+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<aq8cd5Fp5viU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pretty sure Bill Gunshannon would like to see this...

http://news.techworld.com/personal-tech/3434094/universities-fail-offer-essential-programming-skills-like-cobol/

I think this is an appalling article, but the comments (apart from mine) are 
really interesting. Instead of dozens of posts from outraged COBOL guys, a 
different trend is detectable in them.

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: Universities are failing us with COBOL?

- **From:** bill@server1.cs.uofs.edu (Bill Gunshannon)
- **Date:** 2013-03-12T12:50:25+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<aq8mkhFqmbjU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<aq8cd5Fp5viU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
In article <aq8cd5Fp5viU1@mid.individual.net>,
	"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> writes:
> Pretty sure Bill Gunshannon would like to see this...
> 

It was interesting and for the most part I agree.

> http://news.techworld.com/personal-tech/3434094/universities-fail-offer-essential-programming-skills-like-cobol/
> 
> I think this is an appalling article, 

Why?  I thought it was pretty good.  Until I go to the next to last
paragraph.  No bias there, right?  :-)

>                                        but the comments (apart from mine) are 
> really interesting. Instead of dozens of posts from outraged COBOL guys, a 
> different trend is detectable in them.

Comments?  First, they show a quite a lack of understanding about the
current state of "mainframe" computing.  There are no character cell
terminals hooked up to IBM zSystems (were there ever? :-)  JCL?  It is
no different than a Unix Shell or VMS' DCL.  

Your comments are as would be expected, but some of the others are
just plain funny.  Oh, and the comment about paychecks reminded me
of yet another very large system written in COBOL that is very
unlikely (if even possible) to be re-written.  DOD Payroll.  That is
every military member of the Army, Navy, Air Force, Marine Corps,
Coast Guard, all the civilians for those branches, and for the DOD
it self and, all the retirees.  That's 3.2 millioon military, 2 million
military retirees, 800,000 civilians and civilian retirees the number
of which I coldnot find at that moment.  That's a payroll of well
over 6 million, almost 5% of the population.  Care to try doing that
while maintaining a functioning system with no disruptions in service?
Taxes, payrools, insurance, credit cards, mortgages, like it or not,
COBOL is not going away.  Not in my lifetime, not in your lifetime
and probably not in our offsprings lifetimes either.  Isn't it time
people accepted this fact and took it back into the IT fold?  I have
never said that COBOL is the only thing that should be taught in
schools, but by the same token it is a grave dis-service to both the
students and the industry to pretend it is insignificant and that it
will just go away.

bill
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Universities are failing us with COBOL?

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2013-03-12T16:25:25+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<khnktl$m2n$1@reader2.panix.com>`

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Universities are failing us with COBOL?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>

```
Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> In article <aq8cd5Fp5viU1@mid.individual.net>,
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> writes:
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> no different than a Unix Shell or VMS' DCL.

Yes, I agree the article was written by someone with little understanding of 
mainframe reality. But you would expect a senior Micro Focus guy to make 
more pertinent comments and he didn't. I think it was appalling because 
there were no cited references for the "statistics", the "poll" was carried 
out by Micro Focus who are sponsoring COBOL in some Universities and have an 
obvious vested interest in keeping it in the news, and it simply 
regurgitated stuff that has already been discredited. ITo me, it was a 
biased poll which claimed to be "International" but was actually based 
largely on Micro Focus sites (amazingly 46% of them reckoned COBOL was going 
nowhere...). And then add to that the old Gartner chestnuts... it really 
doesn't have much credibility for COBOL.

I know it seems like I'm anti-COBOL (I'm not...) but I want to see people 
recognize WHY COBOL is no longer the #1 tool that it was. It doesn't mean 
COBOL is useless or innately flawed, just that we face a different 
environment in 2013 than we did in 1963, and we have different and more 
powerful technology (both hardware and software) to address that with.

>
> Your comments are as would be expected, but some of the others are
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> while maintaining a functioning system with no disruptions in service?

We have a political scandal going on here over a payroll package called 
"Novopay". It was picked up by the current Government to manage the payment 
of teachers. The results are a disaster, with some teachers not being paid 
for 6 months. It seems to me that it was implemented politically and not 
professionally but I'll be discussing it with a cabinet minister who happens 
to be the local MP for Tauranga, over lunch tomorrow, so I hope he has some 
insights as to what went wrong.

> Taxes, payrools, insurance, credit cards, mortgages, like it or not,
> COBOL is not going away.  Not in my lifetime, not in your lifetime
> and probably not in our offsprings lifetimes either.

But it actually IS, Bill. Have you seen no sites drop it in your career? I 
have seen many. And I'm involved with others who are currently trying to 
move on from it. (interestingly, NONE of the PRIMA clients are in NZ...they 
are spread around the world.) Maybe it is different in the USA. I would be 
very surprised to find ANY major computer processing in NZ using COBOL, and 
that includes Banks, Insurance Companies, the National airline, and the 
Stock Market. I worked for some major Banks in Europe and although COBOL was 
a big part of their lives at the time, I believe that is no longer the case 
and their systems are now being networked.

> Isn't it time
> people accepted this fact and took it back into the IT fold?  I have
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> bill

Glad you enjoyed the article, Bill. (I thought of you when I read it :-))

I like your last sentence above and the word "insignificant" made me pause. 
I would readily agree that COBOL is not "insignificant" at the moment, and, 
as such, there is probably some benefit for kids to learn it. My problem 
comes when you get people with an obvious vested interest trying to talk it 
up and claim it is "the future" when it obviously isn't.

It is seriously wrong to steer young people in the wrong direction. The days 
of a career in COBOL are over. That doesn't mean that knowing it has no 
value, but it DOES mean you can no longer base your primary income on it, as 
some of us did for many years.

Pete.
```

```
>On Wed, 13 Mar 2013 10:41:28 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
><dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[104 more quoted lines elided]…
>total?


Back in the early eighties, there were some 50,000 mainframes
installed in the world, now there are under 10,000 (in about half that
many sites).  And half of the total are in banking/financial and
insurance.  And most of the other (non-IBM) mainframes and minis that
used to run a bunch of Cobol are mostly just gone (yes, there are bits
here and there, and the one mini still kicking pretty hard, the
AS/400, was a hotbed of RPG, not really Cobol).  Unless you know of
some Cobol strongholds outside the mainframe, that leaves a few
thousand companies running significant Cobol stacks directly in the
world, with some running some via outsourcers, or embedded in
purchased packages.  Yes, many of those are big.
```

```
to be "re-written" or "ported" anywhere.
```

```
Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> In article <pl00k896ta2horeonamk2bvtg6vbv71tpd@4ax.com>,
> Robert Wessel <robertwessel2@yahoo.com> writes:
…[152 more quoted lines elided]…
> mean no one is running COBOL on non-mainframe boxes.

Absolutely! I have been surprised by the people who contact PRIMA because 
they have COBOL systems running OFF mainframes. We are not currently 
offering a full solution for mainframe sites (although we do offer Data 
Conversion from indexed files to normalized RDB), because the mainframe 
COBOL doesn't fully support OO (AFAIK, if anybody has different information 
on this, please let me know...), which is needed for our Data Access Layer. 
Implementation of a mainframe solution is in our TODO stack but it is 
unlikely to happen for some time. (It will probably be implemented with Open 
COBOL, as a base system, when we do do it...) I'm resisting this because 
there are other ways to implement the Data Access Layer which would be ideal 
for a mainframe site, but they require a very large effort to develop on our 
part... :-))
>
>>                                                that leaves a few
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> unlikely to be "re-written" or "ported" anywhere.

We could argue the "likelihood". (I believe it is an inreasing 
probability...:-))

"size" and "reliability" are no longer the exclusive domain of the 
mainframe.  "Scalability" means size is irrelevant and top end servers are 
just as reliable as mainframes in terms of MTBF.

Each new generation of server improves both scalability and reliability, not 
to mention performance...

NASA have decommissioned their last mainframe... that's good enough for 
me... :-)

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Mainframes was Re: Universities are failing us with COBOL?

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** Clark F Morris <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca>
- **Date:** 2013-03-13T22:08:48-03:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7u72k8lf9b090a0u5vnpjnc51og2ub9snc@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<aq8cd5Fp5viU1@mid.individual.net> <aq8mkhFqmbjU1@mid.individual.net> <aq9loaF46pdU1@mid.individual.net> <8cpvj8hlga60g2q6k0ap3nb4374rctcolr@4ax.com> <pl00k896ta2horeonamk2bvtg6vbv71tpd@4ax.com> <aqbcfuFf9kaU1@mid.individual.net> <aqcfkcFndv8U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Thu, 14 Mar 2013 12:15:24 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>> much snipped
>
>"size" and "reliability" are no longer the exclusive domain of the 
>mainframe.  "Scalability" means size is irrelevant and top end servers are 
>just as reliable as mainframes in terms of MTBF.

The major problem with the IBM z series is that it doesn't scale low
enough.  While a large percentage of the IBM mainframes are in the
United States, there are large numbers of non-US customers including
at least a few new ones.  Since many (most?) installations don't go to
the effort needed for true reliability and 24/7 operation regardless
of platform I would want look at shops that have implemented for 24/7
virtually no down time on operations where data base integrity and
exact results are required.  Google's search operations don't need to
meet the requirement.  I am skeptical of claims on both sides of the
fence.  I do know that the memory checking is a lot stronger on the z
series than it is on my Windows7 Intel computer and there are other
checks built into the z series that are not on my home computer to
guarantee accurate, repeatable results.  Similar things can well be on
server class Intel and boxes of other architecture but I would have to
see the manuals.  
>
>Each new generation of server improves both scalability and reliability, not 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>me... :-)

Given that most of NASA's needs are for scientific computing, I am
surprised that they lasted this long since there are decent commercial
business packages and COBOLs for most of the RISC based systems.

Clark Morris
>
```

```
While there is no "BC" version of the EC12 yet, the z114 scaled down
to 26 MIPS, which is pretty darn small.  Of course IBM still charges
~$100K for that (sans peripherals).  OTOH, if you're a developer, you
can get a several-hundred MIPS emulated box for a few thousand
dollars.


>I do know that the memory checking is a lot stronger on the z
>series than it is on my Windows7 Intel computer and there are other
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…

Sure, it's vastly better than on a desktop system, but only moderately
better than on proper server systems.  Server class x86 systems
support extensive ECC, chipkill, and all that.  Not quite up to
mainframe standards, but enough that it doesn't make all that much
practical difference.

For an example, see chapter seven of:

http://www.intel.com/content/dam/www/public/us/en/documents/datasheets/xeon-e7-8800-4800-2800-families-vol-2-datasheet.pdf
```

```
The "article" is interesting but the some comments were written not to
discredit Cobol, some who commented really lacks the knowledge of
Cobol. Even some Cobol programmers (almost always mainframe Cobol
programmers) "thinks" that Cobol is not present on the cloud... IT IS
NOW. I did a pure Cobol (COM+ back-end) online solutions using ASP/
Javascript/Ajax/jQuery and it works perfectly. My platform language is
Cobol (even database) and my presentation scripting layer are ASP/Ajax/
jQuery.

People are NOT against Cobol language per se, but it is how it is
presented... it is always marketed that it is for the maintenance of
legacy codes only. Definitely not, online solutions today has Cobol on
it. Move on people! Cobol still exist!

Question is: Are these Cobol vendor fair? Guess not. Cobol compilers
are too much costly and that is the ONLY issue for me.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Universities are failing us with COBOL?

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-03-14T12:31:29+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<aqcgiiFnkggU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<aq8cd5Fp5viU1@mid.individual.net> <aq8mkhFqmbjU1@mid.individual.net> <aq9loaF46pdU1@mid.individual.net> <8cpvj8hlga60g2q6k0ap3nb4374rctcolr@4ax.com> <014c2155-fd04-49cb-a9bd-9a24e5584da6@m9g2000pbb.googlegroups.com>`

```
iNFO_rene wrote:
> The "article" is interesting but the some comments were written not to
> discredit Cobol, some who commented really lacks the knowledge of
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> are too much costly and that is the ONLY issue for me.

Like Rene, I also use ASP (actually ASP.NET)/Ajax/jQuery  for Web 
Development. Along with HTML5/CSS3 and fallbacks for older Browsers. COBOL 
can certainly be used in this environment. (I used to use it for CGI and 
ISAPI until I realised there were more powerful (FREE) tools available.)

I have often remarked here that it isn't that you CAN'T do it in COBOL... 
you certainly CAN. (Well, mostly...)

BUT, there are better ways.

What do I mean by "better"?  Quicker to write (with FREE tools that generate 
much of it for you), MUCH less code (because the code-behind language is 
more powerful than COBOL), and easier to maintain (partly because there is 
less of it, but also because true OO languages just make invoking objects 
and layers so much simpler than COBOL does.)

But, best of all, these solutions are FREE and so is help with learning them 
and solving problems you encounter along the way, with sample code and help 
a mouse click away and also free.

Another aspect of this which isn't immediately obvious, is that if you have 
better tools it empowers you to do more. I am currently undertaking a HUGE 
web development that involves many hours of work and "pushing the envelope". 
I wouldn't even have attempted it in COBOL...

Pete.
```

```
Over the last 25 years, the sites I personally know about who moved on from
```

```
On 3/12/2013 5:41 PM, Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Bill Gunshannon wrote:
>> In article <aq8cd5Fp5viU1@mid.individual.net>,
…[99 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I would be the first to acknowledge that COBOL has some minor 
idiosyncrasies (cough) BUT can anyone seriously think that it *must* 
take a long time to learn enough about COBOL to be able to support 
routine maintenance operations?

Contrast that with maintaining a huge kettle of Java code or C.

Of course, people will wail and wring their hands saying "but, what 
about AP101A - that beast of a program that the old bugger Snarfo wrote 
30 years ago and none of us can understand..." as counter example, but 
what I mean is that a great deal of the average COBOL programs in use in 
business can be understood without extensive knowledge of COBOL itself.

The nuances of C and Java and the complexity of the operating system 
environment(s) in which that code runs makes them much more difficult 
practical workplaces.

I don't mean to diminish the potential complexity of COBOL systems (I 
have seen many collections of trash that should never have been referred 
to as a "system"...) but seriously: a full semester [or more?] of just 
COBOL??
```

```
Kerry Liles wrote:
> On 3/12/2013 5:41 PM, Pete Dashwood wrote:
>> Bill Gunshannon wrote:
…[122 more quoted lines elided]…
> more?] of just COBOL??

This is a really important point.

A properly trained "programmer" with a decent grounding in the concepts and 
knowledge of a couple of modern programming languages, maybe some scripting 
also, can EASILY pick up enough COBOL knowledge to be able to maintain 
WELL-WRITTEN COBOL. (If it ISN'T well-written, then even COBOL experts may 
have trouble with it...)

Unfortunately, the converse is NOT true. You generally can't take someone 
who has only ever known COBOL and let them loose on Java or C#, for example, 
and expect a result any time soon... They are bewildered, because they have 
not received exposure to the concepts they need to have, by ONLY learning 
COBOL. Sadly, because COBOL is "easy" (in the sense that it is familair and 
English-like) practitioners can come to believe over time that it is the 
"only way" and that once they've mastered COBOL that is all they need to 
know.

Said it before and I say it again: "Expand your toolset; learn more if you 
intend to be a profesional programmer."

Pete.
```

---

```
now.  John's final words, when he died in Ephesus at around 100 years of 
age, the ONLY one of the Apostles NOT to come to an unpleasant end, were a 
repetition of his Master's message: "Love one another."

It seems to me that if all Religions taught that, and all their adherents 
practised it, the world would be a better place.

Pete.
```

## Thread: IT's Record of Failure Is Worse Than You Think — 128 msgs

### IT's Record of Failure Is Worse Than You Think

- **From:** Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2013-03-16T09:42:01-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<f60b7e17-b33b-4103-9378-b8c2ef17753d@googlegroups.com>`

```
< http://www.insurancetech.com/management-strategies/its-record-of-failure-is-worse-than-you/240150837?itc=edit_in_body_cross >

A feel-good story if ever there was one! <Not!>

"Wander ruefully concludes from his research that IT doesn’t
 perform well, that large projects fail or underperform
 regularly and that often the only debate is not whether
 they will be a success or a failure, but to what degree
 they will fail."
```

#### ↳ Re: IT's Record of Failure Is Worse Than You Think

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
- **Date:** 2013-03-16T12:41:20-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<JeOdnezeArX0M9nMnZ2dnUVZ_rGdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<f60b7e17-b33b-4103-9378-b8c2ef17753d@googlegroups.com>`

```
Rick Smith wrote:
> <
> http://www.insurancetech.com/management-strategies/its-record-of-failure-is-worse-than-you/240150837?itc=edit_in_body_cross
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>  they will fail."

Recommend "Systemantics - Why Systems Fail".

As it turns out, there are "laws," some of which are:

* A large system designed from scratch will not work and cannot be made to 
work. You have to start over with a smaller, working, system.

* Malfunction, or even total non-function, of a large system may not be 
detectable for a long time. If ever.

* Fail-safe systems often fail by failing to fail safe.

* Humans within a system do not do what persons with the same title do in 
the real world.

and more...
```

#### ↳ Re: IT's Record of Failure Is Worse Than You Think

```
Rick Smith wrote:
> <
> http://www.insurancetech.com/management-strategies/its-record-of-failure-is-worse-than-you/240150837?itc=edit_in_body_cross
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>  they will fail."

The keywords here are "LARGE projects".

Many of us learned years ago that "small is beautiful".

LARGE IT projects should be broken into "chunks" and the chunks should be 
developed as separate projects.

I have made it a policy to keep IT projects under $20 million and this has 
worked very well for me, even in corporations where the original assessment 
was much larger than that.

See my comments back on the link posted above.

Pete.
```

```
"The controllers office is planning to return its payroll
 operations back to the legacy system 'that is currently

(For those who don't recall, the California payroll system
is written in COBOL and was the focus of political infighting
between the Governor and the state Controller.)

What I found interesting in that article, relating to the
subject, was,

"'There are one of two possibilities here, the data
 getting into the system is wrong, or the system itself
 is taking correct data and mangling it,' said Michael
 Krigsman, CEO of consulting firm Asuret. 'Despite SAP's
 history with project failures, SAP has many other
 government customers using payroll successfully. In this
 case, it's just not likely that SAP would not be able
 to handle a fairly simple payroll for 1,300 people.'"

"California also has a history of large IT project failures,
 which 'further casts questions onto the role of the state
 controller's office in this,' Krigsman added."

"Sooner or later, COBOL will kill us all"

"I read with some trepidation that the computer language
 COBOL (Short for COmmon Business Oriented Language) is
 fifty years old today."

...

"So now, dear reader, you know our dirty secret. Large
 parts of the software eco system that sustains our critical
 infrastructure is written in a language that practically
 no one knows, almost no one can fix, and is effectively
 immortal."

These beg the question: Are there some systems, written in
COBOL, so large and intractable, that projects to 'modernize'
them are doomed to failure?
```

```
Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> In article <1b08fcf3-73d8-42e8-8afe-64992780dce3@googlegroups.com>,
> Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com> writes:
…[69 more quoted lines elided]…
> bill

Just to be clear Bill:

You are saying that both of the above systems in the United States can NEVER 
be replaced and will process in COBOL for evermore?

Obviously, that is unlikely.

So how long then? 20 years? 30 years? 50 years?  What?

If you think about this you will realise that our point of difference is 
just about the time scale. (Unless you want to affirm the first question 
asked above.. which says NEVER...)

So, if we agree they will be replaced at some point, what are the factors 
that have a bearing on that?

Mostly time and resource, coupled with the fact that they are generally 
working fine so there is no reason to replace them.

However, I think there is an "evolutionary" solution that will see these 
systems gradually replaced, rather than an overnight migration. Little bits 
of them will be chipped away by new technology and eventually there will be 
very little left of the existing COBOL core. While it would be extremely 
difficult to fund and implement a complete replacement as a single project, 
it is not so hard to replace minor sub-systems, maybe on a local basis.

Please see my comments on Data based migration elsewhere in this thread.

Pete.
```

```
looking and discussing the opinion of all involved was to not bid because
it was a guaranteed losing business venture.  Too big, too complex, and
too tightly tied to the current architecture.  It is not that the IRS is
so in love with the current system, it has become the marriage made in
hell and the devil is not interested in a divorce.  Oh, did I mention,
it's a UNISYS 2200 shop with a history going back to the 1100 days.
COBOL, DMS11 and, yes, a bit of Univac Assembler thrown in for good
measure.

DFAS, on the other hand, is an IBM shop and like most IBM shops just has
no interest in changing anything.
```

```
'Everything is a matter of degree' is absolutely true!  Here's a situation 
to ponder:

Person A says 'You will never see (x) happen.'

Person B says 'You will see (x) happen within (n) years.'

At (n - 1) years (x) has not happened and Person B dies.

At (n - .5) years (x) happens.

Give that in English 'you' addresses both definite and indefinite numbers 
of people... are you correct?

DD
```

```
On Sunday, March 17, 2013 2:14:05 PM UTC-4, Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> In article <1b08fcf3-73d8-42e8-8afe-64992780dce3@googlegroups.com>,
> 
> 	Rick Smith <rs847...@gmail.com> writes:
[snip]
> 
> > These beg the question: Are there some systems, written in
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Followed very closely by The Defence Fiance and Accounting System (DFAS).

Here is some information on the IRS:
< http://www.theregister.co.uk/2013/02/25/unisys_irs_mainframe_support_deal/ >

"National tax collection agencies were among the first
 organizations in the world to use IBM, Sperry, Burroughs,
 and other mainframes, and despite all the grumbling and
 grousing over the high costs of these venerable box, they
 have invested a fortune in COBOL applications and it is not
 so easy to ditch the mainframe as it might seem. And it
 looks like the mainframe will be sticking around the US
 Internal Revenue Service for quite a bit longer, if a new
 support contract won by Unisys, worth a potential $650m
 over the next decade, is any indication."

NOTE: 'next decade' in the above!

"The IRS installed its first such sequential database – called
 Master File – on IBM systems back in 1962, which predate the
 launch of the System/360 mainframe by several years. This
 batch-oriented, sequential database is whacked by COBOL
 applications and was only able to do weekly updates to
 taxpayer information. CADE was able to do daily updates and
 was planned to be rolling out during the second quarter of
 the US government's fiscal 2012, which is about a year ago.
 CADE 2 was an improved version of this database."

"So how much iron does it take to collect taxes from all of
 the individuals and business in the United States? Not as much
 as you might think. The IRS data center in Martinsburg has two
 System zEnterprise 196 mainframes, one with 8,800 MIPS and the
 other with 8,000 MIPS. The top-end zEnterprise has north of
 52,000 MIPS and costs somewhere around $28.6m, so this pair of
 sysplexed machines can failover for each other. The
 Martinsburg machines run the old Master File as well as the
 CADE system and were slated to run CADE 2 as well, and
 interestingly, shifting to CADE 2 was going to free up about
 8,000 MIPS of capacity on these core IBM machines.
 (Presumably through the retirement of Master File.) The
 Memphis data center had an IBM System z9 mainframe with a mere
 900 MIPS, but this box was supposed to be upgraded last year
 to a System zEnterprise 196 to provide remote failover for the
 West Virginia machines."

There appears to be a discrepancy in the above, one machine
has 8,800 MIPS, the other 8,000 MIPS; but shifting to
CADE 2 will "free up 8,000 MIPS"? To me that suggests that
the IRS has been running without a backup system!

In any case, it does suggest the IRS is planning to remain
with mainframes and COBOL for at least ten more years.

The National Finance Center may be another to add to your
list.
< http://fcw.com/articles/2011/09/12/feat-shared-services-private-cloud.aspx?sc_lang=en >

"NSSC certainly isn’t unique. The Agriculture Department’s
 National Finance Center (NFC) provides central payroll
 services for the agency, and another 170 departments in
 all three branches of government tap into the resource,
 which issues paychecks to about 655,000 employees — or
 35 percent of the civilian workforce — every two weeks."

"Finally, although service providers strive for the cost
 advantages of standardized services, they still need a
 pricing plan that accommodates any special requirements
 that individual customers might need. For example, White
 said NFC might need to manage wage garnishments on
 employees’ paychecks."

"'There’s also reporting that agencies can do off our
 reporting center, and they need CPU minutes on our
 mainframe to do that,' he said. 'This is tracked and
 charged on a usage basis.'"

So NFC runs payroll for 655,000 employees on a mainframe,
which suggests, to me, another large COBOL user.
```

```
 projects. The company recently completed the first phase of
 a four-year project with the U.S. Army Materiel Command to
 decommission dozens of systems used for managing logistics
 and replace them with a single instance of SAP. That project
 had some of the hallmarks of IRS modernization: decades-old
 technology and business processes; a workforce set in its
 ways. But the Army’s project managers got the job done by
 relentlessly courting end users and by insisting that CSC
 would not get paid unless it met an exhaustive set of
 performance metrics. In other words, the Army actively
 managed the project, which is something the IRS failed
 to do."

"Can this project be saved? 'People ask, Is modernization
 going to fail?' says Levitan. 'I say we can’t let it fail.
 This program must succeed for the welfare of the entire
 federal government.'"

(Of course, I must question whether the government or the
```

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <aqrnhlF4fb8U1@mid.individual.net>,
> Bill Gunshannon <billg999@cs.uofs.edu> wrote:
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> Some might say that this speaks well for the process.

I agree. Democracy is far from perfect ("perfection" would involve me being 
King of the World...), but I also take Bill's point. If you only have bad 
options to choose from, it is almost as bad as having NO choice.

People can FEEL disenfranchised (even when, in fact, they are not).

At this point what is supposed to happen is that you should run for office 
or persuade someone who you think would be suitable, to run for office, and 
support them.

(But it is much easier to sit back and grizzle about the system... Besides, 
moaning about the stupidity of the Government is a National pastime in most 
countries and assists the selling of large quantities of beer, which is good 
for the economy...)

We seldom get the politicians we would like, but we invariably end up 
getting the politicians we deserve.

Sometimes, people who would be eminently suitable do run for office. 
Sometimes they even get elected.

But after a while in office they change. ("Power corrupts...") Sometimes it 
isn't even that power corrupts, rather it is the long hours away from family 
and the frustration of dealing with bureaucrats over long periods of time 
that knocks the initial enthusiasm out of them. Constantly uinder attack, 
knowing that whatever you do, somebody won't like it...

Very occasionally you get someone who is really passionate about service and 
they do actually make a difference.

I think the percentage of people who run for office just because they want 
their snouts in the trough is actually very low, but it is impossible to 
quantitfy.

I'd like to think that most politicians start out genuinely wanting to do 
something worthwhile; the percentage that actually achieve this is lower 
than we would like.

Pete.
```

- **References:** `<f60b7e17-b33b-4103-9378-b8c2ef17753d@googlegroups.com> <aqr8flF12mfU1@mid.individual.net> <kia5sv$rt7$1@reader2.panix.com> <aqrnhlF4fb8U1@mid.individual.net> <kiah8n$4ob$1@reader2.panix.com> <aqs7o0F885gU1@mid.individual.net> <aqtnioFhk9bU2@mid.individual.net> <aqv038Fqu2fU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Thu, 21 Mar 2013 12:46:46 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>Bill Gunshannon wrote:
>> In article <aqs7o0F885gU1@mid.individual.net>,
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>>> But after a while in office they change. ("Power corrupts...")


At least in the U.S., there are several styles of government for a
village/town/city type of entity.  The "city" style of government
(which is not limited to entities officially called "cities"), has a
mayor as executive and a legislative council, the members of which are
usually called aldermen or, less frequently, councilors.  In NZ I
think you call that structure a city council.

A common alternative structure, especially for smaller* municipalities
is the "village" structure, where there is a village board instead,
and the president of the board (often actually titled "mayor", even
though that person does not actually hold that sort of executive
function), has no particular special power, except that of agenda and
tie-breaking.


*Although there's no hard-and-fast rule - the town I live in has a
village style government, but there's a town 10 miles east of here a
third the size of my town, but with a city style government.
```

```
Sorry, Pete but I can not remember. I doubt if I accurately wrote it down either.
```

```
[snip]

>People can FEEL disenfranchised (even when, in fact, they are not).

In the Oldene Dayse it was taught that facts were to be considered first; 
perhaps times have changed.

I once read an article which described a conference about 
Anti-Intellectualism in America (or something like that) at which speakers 
voiced their grave concerns about persecution of those who Thought 
Differently.  One of the speakers was Guenter Grass; among many other 
things about him it can be said that he lived through a time of 
persecution of those who Thought Differently.

Mr Grass' speech was described as even-handed, measured and slightly 
cautionary.  The speaker who followed him denigrated Mr Grass' statement b 
by describing it as a 'bar mitzvah speech'; the firebrand then whipped the 
audience into a frenzy by describing how they were being threatened with 
imprisonment, torment and torture because they Thought Differently... the 
police were at the door!

Mr Grass mentioned that he had lived in times when the police would have 
already broken down the doors and handcuffed the participants... but 
perhaps the police in America were very slow.

DD
```

```
On Tuesday, March 26, 2013 10:31:33 AM UTC-4, docd...@panix.com wrote:
> In article <4744107f-7114-499a-acf7-b2b67a5a1937@googlegroups.com>,
> Rick Smith  <rs847...@gmail.com> wrote:
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> 'Who wrote this crap?  Oops...'

About two weeks ago, I deleted a bunch of my code: COBOL, C, and
Visual Basic; that I determined was no longer of any value
for what I wanted to do for the next few years. The oldest was
a COBOL program from 1989. While it may have made fifteen years,
I didn't look at before deleting it.

I have no reason to believe, now, that four programs I wrote
ten days ago will survive even one year.

I also have more than 250,000 lines of COBOL, C, Microsoft assembler,
Basic, Visual Basic, C++, Motorola 6800 assembler, and Intel 8051
embedded C. Most was written by others: the COBOL, C, Microsoft
assembler, and BASIC; which I maintained, including Y2K. The company,
having been sold and no questions having been asked about the
code in ten years, I think it about time to clean up my disk.
[The Pascal MT+ and Z80 assembler code, I wrote, never made it off
the CP/M system to my PC.]

By far the worst of the code for understanding was the COBOL, with
its large procedure divisions, PERFORM THRU, copy-paste-modify code
and seemingly arbitrary control flow.

Thus, my 'feels' right avoids everything I found objectionable in
the COBOL code I had to maintain. It is much easier for me to live
with.
```

```
>In article <kiah8n$4ob$1@reader2.panix.com>,
>	docdwarf@panix.com () writes:

>Is that supposed to be an insult?

No more - or less - than referring to one's fellow-citizens as 'sheep' or 
accusing a voter of 'sleeping through the last election' might be.

>If so, way to subtle.  I don't get it.

There are people, Mr Gunshannon, who claim to love the Constitution of the 
United States of America and would have the franchise to vote restricted 
to those who have completed a period of military enlistment.  Such a 
restriction is utterly at odds with the Constitution which they claim to 
love.

There are people who claim to love the Constitution of the United States 
of America and would, based on an argument of conflicting self-interest, 
remove the franchise to vote from anyone who receives a check from the 
Federal government.  Such a restriction is also at odds etc.

The United States of America is a young, rich, vibrant and variegated 
creature.  Her people, likewise, are of many stripes.  To dismiss 
differing views as those of sheep or sleepers is repugnant to me and at 
odds with the foundations of my country; it smacks of whining, 
self-indulgent sore-loserism.

DD
```

```
Rick Smith wrote:
> On Wednesday, March 20, 2013 7:11:07 PM UTC-4, Bill Gunshannon wrote:
>> In article <kid7ed$ht8$1@reader2.panix.com>,
…[44 more quoted lines elided]…
> having voted in any election since 1987.

In some "Democratic" countries (Australia, for example), it is required by 
law to vote (you can legally spoil your paper).

There is talk of doing it here as well.

I have never forgotten an election here back in the 60s when someone, under 
cover of darkness, risked life and limb to suspend themselves from the 
Newmarket viaduct in Auckland so they could write: "Don't vote. The 
Government will get in." in large letters for the early morning commuters to 
see on their way to work.

Sadly, there is an element of truth in it.

Whatever Government you get (certainly, in a small country) will not be SO 
different from the one you DIDN'T get.

ANY government is constrained by certain factors which dictate things like 
Economic policy and Foreign policy, so despite all the pre-election 
tub-thumping, there is a limit to what they can actually do, once elected.

Someone sent me the following link yesterday, which I think has to rank as 
one of the very best political speeches I have ever seen:

http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=94lW6Y4tBXs

Straight talking common sense. Too many governments are slavishly following 
Keynesian principles and trying to borrow themselves out of trouble. It is 
good to see someone saying: "Enough is enough...." Unless we stimulate 
productivity, any growth is going to be short-lived. Growth based on 
borrowing is simply an illusion.

I'm pleased to see the NZ economy is going in the right direction again, but 
the drought we are currently experiencing will have a delayed effect on 
productivity in the agricultural sector.

We are still recovering from the devastating effects of Christchurch, but 
even this has caused growth as the repairs are undertaken. It is indeed an 
ill wind that blows nobody any good...

Pete.
```

- **Date:** 2013-03-21T13:21:51+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

- **References:** `<f60b7e17-b33b-4103-9378-b8c2ef17753d@googlegroups.com> <aqrnhlF4fb8U1@mid.individual.net> <kiah8n$4ob$1@reader2.panix.com> <aqtmugFhk9bU1@mid.individual.net> <kicqq4$mdj$1@reader2.panix.com> <aquf3tFmv67U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> In article <kicqq4$mdj$1@reader2.panix.com>,
> docdwarf@panix.com () writes:
…[75 more quoted lines elided]…
> bill

Bill, as someone with no axe to grind regarding American politics, I 
honestly think you are being too cynical. You have to give "people" some 
credit. I'm sure both you and Doc Dwarf are staunch patriots who care deeply 
about the USA and your loyalty to your country is not in question.

I'm also pretty sure that many of the things you are complaining about do 
have some basis in fact, and many people prefer not to be actively involved 
in politics so they "go with the flow". I don't think it makes them sheep, 
but your opinion about that is as valid as mine.

In the original "Democracy" people voted and if they lost, they accepted the 
decision of the majority, knowing they would get another chance at a future 
date and could spend time trying to persuade people in the interim. We seem 
to have lost that and there are groups now who WON'T accept the majority 
decision but would rather disrupt the whole society with coercion and 
terror, until they are either killed (along with any number of innocent 
bystanders) or their demands are met.

There is much to be despondent about in the politics of Democracy, but it is 
still the best system that has been devised in thousands of years.

Personally, I try to stay apolitical. I vote whichever way I think will be 
best for myself and the community I live in. (My lunch with Simon was not 
about politics and with the 5 minute exception of the Novopay fiasco, we 
never discussed anything political.)

If I saw the blackness that you are seeing, and felt the impotence that you 
are feeling, I would probably move to a different location. (Maybe even a 
different country...)

If you believe you have no options, if you believe the population are 
mindless sheep and the system is so corrupt it cannot be fixed, then you are 
indeed going to be very unhappy.

If you take a closer look, you may be surprised. For example, let's say the 
candidate you wanted wasn't elected. How much DIRECT impact on your life has 
that event actually had? Are you seriously disadvantaged because a different 
candidate got in? Doesn't the new candidate have to deal with the same 
basics that your candidate would have? There are constraints on ALL 
politicians, no matter what their party colour is.

It probably isn't as serious to have "lost" as you might think. I can think 
of several occasions, living in different countries, where the Government I 
wanted wasn't elected and the Leader was definitely not who I would have 
chosen, and yet, they turned out not to be SO bad...and in at least one 
case, I was agreeably surprised... :-)

Pete.
```

```
On Monday, March 18, 2013 1:12:01 PM UTC-4, Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> In article <b04295fd-c56e-4114-9e24-3dcc816fb9b6@googlegroups.com>,
> 	Rick Smith <rs847...@gmail.com> writes:
[snip]

> > "Can this project be saved? 'People ask, Is modernization
> >  going to fail?' says Levitan. 'I say we can't let it fail.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> a non-starter.

From the context, as I understand it, the IRS.

With a better idea of what to look for, I found this article,
< http://www.govexec.com/magazine/features/2012/04/fixing-irs/41637/ >
Article dated: April 1, 2012.

"For decades the Internal Revenue Service tried but failed
 to modernize its massive data systems, but Doug Shulman
 finally has something to celebrate. When he became IRS
 commissioner in 2008, Shulman set out to rectify a key
 technology project, which had fallen years behind schedule
 and was tens of millions of dollars over budget. Now the
 goal that eluded the agency for more than 20 years—the
 conversion of taxpayer data to a modernized database—has
 become a reality."
 
"On Jan. 17, the IRS flipped the switch on its new Customer
 Account Data Engine, known as CADE 2, moving the data of
 140 million taxpayers to a 21st century computing environment.
 Successor to the failed CADE 1 project, the new system allows
 the agency to update taxpayer accounts and process returns
 on a daily basis rather than weekly, which was the case with
 the Individual Master File [IMF]."

"CADE 2, in contrast [to IMF], is a relational database in
 which accounts can be accessed directly."

"Technology issues are at the core of the IRS’ modernization
 effort. The agency’s first major attempt to update its data
 systems began in 1988. But Congress cut off funding for the
 program in 1995 after concluding that much of the $3.5 billion
 spent had been wasted. In 1998, under heightened oversight
 from Congress, Rossotti launched the Business Systems
 Modernization [BSM] program in which the IRS partnered with
 a consortium of firms led by Computer Sciences Corp. It was
 estimated that BSM, which included 11 information technology
 projects, would take 15 years and $7 billion to complete."

"By the time Shulman became commissioner, CADE 1 had missed
 several key milestones.  The completion date had been pushed
 back from 2013 to sometime between 2018 and 2022. Shulman was
 not prepared to go to Congress and ask for hundreds of
 millions more dollars for a program the fruits of which
 wouldn’t be available for another decade. He and his team made
 two key decisions: focus exclusively on the CADE project to
 the exclusion of other downstream applications included in
 BSM, and bring management of the program in-house."

"Under Shulman’s leadership, the project was turned around.
 'We broke a cycle of batch processing that had been done for
 50 years the same way and that is now being done in a day,'
 Milholland says. 'We’ve taken an amazing amount of waste out
 of the system.'"

"David Powner, GAO’s director of information technology issues,
 describes CADE 2 as 'the key to modernization.'"


A document describing Business Systems Modernization [BSM],
in summary form, 'FOR RELEASE: 1/11/01', is available at,
< http://www.irs.gov/pub/irs-news/fs-01-05.pdf >.

"The IRS network is composed of:
 • 40 mainframe computers,
 • 871 midrange computers,
 • more than 100,000 individual computers,
 • 2,779 vendor supplied software products, and
 • more than 50 million lines of IRS maintained computer code."

"The IRS uses a system called the Enterprise Life Cycle (ELC)
 to plan and manage work throughout BSM. The ELC establishes
 a set of repeatable processes and a system of reviews,
 checkpoints, and milestones that reduce the risks of system
 development. Every product in the ELC process must be reviewed,
 accepted and approved before it moves to the next milestone.
 There are several review processes and strict measurement
 criteria that help reduce risks and ensure even the smallest
 detail isn't overlooked."
```

```
On Sunday, March 17, 2013 2:14:05 PM UTC-4, Bill Gunshannon wrote:
[snip]

> I have siad it before and I will say it again.  The IRS.
> Followed very closely by The Defence Fiance and Accounting System (DFAS).

While the IRS was able to make some changes within its
major failure, particularly the implementation of CADE 2;
one attempted change (DIMHRS) at DFAS had a more spectacular
failure, it just didn't cost as much.

Estimates of source code for DFAS, in 1997, were found at,
< http://www.gao.gov/archive/1997/ai97117.pdf >. This
showed about 60,000,000 lines for Y2K remediation and
testing. Approximately 10,000,000 lines involved military
pay. This was divided across four systems.

An article dated February 21, 2010,
< http://articles.dailypress.com/2010-02-21/news/dp-local_milupdate_0222feb22_1_military-personnel-payroll-navy-and-air-force >

"After $1 billion and 12 years of effort, Defense officials
 have pulled the plug on a hapless plan to bring the four
 military branches under a single, modern payroll and personnel
 records system."

"The object of so much disaffection is the Defense Integrated
 Military Human Resources System (DIMHRS), ..."

"The current systems use programming language from the 1960s
 that are unable to handle complex changes. When new pays are
 adopted, it was taking the Army on average 12 to 18 months
 to automate. Some pays, like medical bonuses, can't be
 programmed and must be calculated manually."

"DIMHRS was to relieve all of that. It would track assignments,
 process orders and show immediate changes to members' duty
 status to ensure timely, accurate pay, benefits and service
 credit."

Another article is available at,
< http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Defense_Integrated_Military_Human_Resources_System >.
This article identifies PeopleSoft as the intended replacement
system.

"In a time-honored tactic, the Army DIMHRS project has been
 renamed to Army Integrated Personnel and Pay System (IPPS).
 All future development, if any, will likely proceed under
 that banner."


I also found a document dated '2002Apr01', for Electronic
Data Systems Corporation (EDS) to provide services for
DFISS II,
< www.dfas.mil/dms/dfas/foia/pdf/22002A0002.pdf >

"DFAS activities are transitioning from maintaining
 mainframe-based COBOL applications to developing Software
 systems operating in client/server and web-based environments.
 This has required the use of new life cycle models (e.g.,
 incremental/prototyping versus waterfall) and development
 methods and techniques (graphical user interface design,
 relational data base design, etc.). Most new development
 efforts will comply with DFAS Corporate Information
 Infrastructure (DCII) standards while requiring the use of
 Computer Aided Software Engineering (CASE) tools, i.e., Oracle
 Designer/Developer in a centrally managed software engineering
 environment."

Apparently DFAS tried to begin moving off COBOL and mainframes
ten years ago, with massive failures and, probably, limited
successes. DFISS III does not mention "transitioning from
maintaining mainframe-based COBOL applications". <g>
```

```
and my civilian.  If you look at my Civilian W2 from last year it

Other than amounts and method of disbursement, there has been very
little change in the general structure of military pay in my entire
time with the Army (1968 until today).  As a COBOL programmer I could
modify the program to handle the case of a benefit that only went to
one individual.  What one receives is clearly spelled out and controlled
by law.  It is not arbitrary.  Anyone can look at the current (and past)
pay scales to see this.

to being in the military that civilian corporations just don't have to
```

```
> the Navy and the Dept. of the Army.

These were systems as they were in 1997. They may have been renamed
in following years. The systems were identified as:

1 DCPS - Defense Civilian Pay System 944,000 LOC
2 DJMS-AC - Defense Joint Military Pay System-Active Component 5,134,069 LOC
3 DJMS-RC - Defense Joint Military Pay System-Reserve Component 1,211,947 LOC
4 MCTFS - Marine Corps Total Force System 2,532,973 LOC

There are other systems for annuitants and retirees, as well.
I said four, though it was more.
```

```
1.  The statement as written is absurd.  The age of the programming
    language has nothing to do with its ability to be modified.
2.  You added a feature that increases the complexity that was not
    stated in the original.  Where did it say this was a "client/server
    environment"?

> 
>>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> be absurd, if it is true. 

It is also unsubstantiated and different from the view of someone who
has actually worked in that envioronment.  I have worked in government
computing environments where complex changes are commonplace and if it
took me 12-18 months to make those changes, I would be looking for another
job.

>                          The second sentence could indicate a limitation in 
> the competence of the people concerned, but that isn't absurd either.

Of course it is.  The supposed incompetence of government workers has
always been a favored joke.  It has no basis in reality.  The government
hires from the same pool of potential employees as the rest of the world
and thus has the same percentage of chance of getting a bad one.  In the
computing field the government pays better than a lot of non-government
employers which means they probably get applications from a lot of the
better ones who are more fit to compete.  So, at worst they are no more
incompetent as the rest of the world and at best they probably have a
much better crop of programmers.  Been there, done that, got the tee shirt.

> 
> You cannot dismiss information as "absurd" simply because it doesn't fit 
> what you want to hear.

No, but I can dismiss it having been there and seen that it is, in fact,
absurd.  No one is more fond of the red herring of the incompetent GS-13
than the contractor who wants to see the government replace them with
contractors.  No conflict of interest there, huh?

> 
> 
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> Yet millions of people around the world are paid by Peoplesoft.

Yeah, but they don;t get things like hazardous duty pay or combat zone
tax exemption or submarine duty pay or any of several dozen things that
are unique to the military.  They offer a canned payroll package that
the customer adapts to, not the other way around.

> 
> We don't know if they ever actually adopted Peoplesoft or what the current 
> status is.

As near as I can tell, HR is till being done the same way it has been
done for at least 10 years and pay the same.  DFAS is still hiring
IBM mainframers to do COBOL and so it Army HRC.

> 
> 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> Not just in Government... :-)

No argument from me on that.  (can you say HP?)

>>
>>>
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> Bill.

How about CASE for a start.  They actually teach that in the graduate
program where I used to (and may again) work.  A hundred different
competing methodologies changing constantly as they fall in and out of
vogue.  Kind of like programming languages.  And then we have "new life
cycle models (e.g., incremental/prototyping versus waterfall)".  The
big push now is Lean Six Sigma and Agile.  All I saw in Agile was a real
good system for hiding failures and succeeding while never actually
delivering anything.  Had to take all the classes when I worked for the
Navy (last year!)  The course started with an explanation of "the way
it used to be done" which was totally bogus.  So, from your classes on
logic, what does it say about your conclussions if the premise from which
they were derived is totally wrong?  OUt of all the Agile classes I had
to take I came away with only one thing that I thought actually worked
and was of value. "Planning Poker"  I keep a bunch of decks of those cards
around now in case I ever get into a place where I need to do some
serious planning involving a number of stake-holders and players.

> 
> You seem to apply it to anything you disagree with, as a form of dismissal, 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> That doesn't mean they are worthless or simply "buzzwords".

Maybe so, but in a world where the soundbite is king throwing around
meaningless buzzwords while providing no real information has become
the norm.  Subjective experience?  I hve way too much.  Especially
where dealing with US governemtn computing is concerned.  I have been
a grunt programmer in the Army, an IT Officer in the Army, a Dept of
the Navy civilian, a Dept of the Army civilian and a governemtn contractor
working for numerous companies with DOD and non-DOD customers.

> 
> The above is very much what the modern approach would require. It seems 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> I'd be interested to know what the current status is.

To the best of my knowledge, DFAS and HRC are continuing as they have for
years.  Modernization is planned, contractors are lining up at the trough
to get their hands on all that free money with no real expectstion of
delivering a product.  In other words, Washington business as usual.  But,
the current budget crisis is likely to put a hold on a lot if not all of
these projects.  And, you know what?  Those COBOL programs will continue
to march deliverig the product they were designed for with little if any
problems.
```

```
On Wednesday, March 20, 2013 7:57:34 AM UTC-4, Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Rick Smith wrote:
> > On Tuesday, March 19, 2013 6:46:23 PM UTC-4, Pete Dashwood wrote:
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> OK, thanks for the c larification.

You're welcome.

Though not associated with DFAS, there was another massive
failure and, at least, five more waiting in the queue.

There was and still is a push to get the US military services
to use commercial off-the-shelf (COTS) software, rather than
custom software and a law requiring that such 'modernization'
be 'auditable' by 2017.

< http://edocs.nps.edu/npspubs/scholarly/theses/2010/Sep/10Sep_Jones_Mark.pdf >,
a Masters Thesis, of 111 pages, describes certain logistics
conversion projects.

"The Army is developing GCSS-Army and the Logistics Modernization
 Program (LMP), the Air Force is developing Expeditionary Combat
 Support System (ECSS) and Defense Enterprise Accounting and
 Management System (DEAMS), the Navy is developing Navy ERP,
 and the Marine Corps is developing GCSS-MC. Each service has had
 difficulties in developing their ERP system."

"Each service’s implementation is designed to modernize business
 systems mainly focusing on logistics management of assets and
 financial tracking of those assets. All use ERP software from
 either Oracle or SAP, but each with different levels of scope
 and functionality designed to replace existing legacy logistics
 IT systems. Many of these legacy systems have been in service
 for over 30 years and support for these systems is becoming scarce."

("Existing Marine Corps IT logistics systems are numerous and many
  are over 30 years old. Support for these antiquated systems is
  disappearing. Several of the Marine Corps ground based logistics
  systems were developed using the Common Business-Oriented Language
  (COBOL) programming language developed in 1959 [8]. One example
  is the Supported Activity Supply System (SASSY). It is a mainframe
  system developed in the 1970s using the COBOL programming language;
  it is still in use today. SASSY is hard to use, contains inaccurate
  data, and does not efficiently support the war fighter [9]. It is
  becoming increasingly difficult to support as the software requires
  much patching and the COBOL SASSY programmers are becoming harder
  to find and maintain in the work force.") [While this is the only
  mention of COBOL, it is implied elsewhere by the use of 'legacy'.]

"The DoD has selected two main suites of software for their
 logistics modernization ERP efforts. The Army and Navy have
 selected SAP and the Air Force and Marine Corps have selected
 the Oracle E-Business Suite."

These programs have estimated life cycle costs of $1 to $3 billion.
Some have had delays of several years. 

One, ECSS, was cancelled at a cost of $1 billion.
< http://www.airforcetimes.com/news/2012/11/fed-how-airforce-blew-one-billion-dud-112612w/ >
```

```
Robert, even in it's heyday (and I know, because I was here) I don't think

that almost everyone here has had a love of COBOL. It is a language which
```

```
On Wednesday, March 20, 2013 8:41:08 PM UTC-4, Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Rick Smith wrote:
[snip]

> > There was and still is a push to get the US military services
> > to use commercial off-the-shelf (COTS) software, rather than
> > custom software and a law requiring that such 'modernization'
> > be 'auditable' by 2017.
[snip]

> > These programs have estimated life cycle costs of $1 to $3 billion.
> > Some have had delays of several years.
[snip]

> However, it also shows they are committed to "modernization" and I believe 
> they will eventually achieve it.

Interpretation! It shows the civilian government is committed to
reigning in the cost of the military. It is an open question as
to whether COTS will work for the military. Thus far the
evidence does not support the use of COTS for 'business
modernization' in the military, though COTS has been used
sucessfully where the DoD's ADA might have been used for
military (weapons) systems.


> I can't see ORACLE or SAP allowing them to fail. It would be disastrous for 
> either company to be associated with such a colossal disaster. Delays, 
> budget overruns, even complete restarts of some projects, can all be 
> forgotten when the solution is delivered and works.

I see Oracle and SAP as blameless. If COTS cannot be used by the
military, then Oracle and SAP might choose to modify their software
to accommodate that which is required, or not. Apparently, this
did happen in the case of PeopleSoft.


> If you look at $3 billion against the total military expenditure, it 
> probably isn't as worrying to the Chiefs of Staff as it might be to mere 
> mortals like us...

Apparently I was not clear. Each of the systems has a discrete cost.
The total cost of the six logistics projects is over $10 billion.
For example, ECSS was $2 billion, but the write-off was $1 billion.
The Personnel and Payroll and, potentially, other 'business
modernization' projects add billions more.


> And you have to believe that some (expensive) lessons have been learned.

Yes, and, likely, the General Accountability Office (GAO) will
write the final reports.


> The bottom line is that the existing COBOL systems WILL be replaced 
> (whatever it takes) and that has been the basic argument of this thread 
> since it started.

The question was whether "projects to 'modernize'" would fail.
However, 'modernize' was never defined; intentionally, I might add.

Two distinct views of 'modernize' emerged. Replacement with COTS
and replacement with other languages. A third, not mentioned, would
be that available with Micro Focus, which would not replace the
COBOL code, entirely. And I am sure both IBM and Unisys would have
some ideas that do not replace the COBOL code, entirely.
```

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()

- **References:** `<f60b7e17-b33b-4103-9378-b8c2ef17753d@googlegroups.com> <e667beee-ed60-42da-97ba-681423fd8c45@googlegroups.com> <aqv396FrisvU1@mid.individual.net> <97f26e91-fd9c-431b-90a2-c034253056ce@googlegroups.com>`

```
In article <97f26e91-fd9c-431b-90a2-c034253056ce@googlegroups.com>,
Rick Smith  <rs847925@gmail.com> wrote:
>On Wednesday, March 20, 2013 8:41:08 PM UTC-4, Pete Dashwood wrote:
>> Rick Smith wrote:
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>military (weapons) systems.

Examining the language might be helpful.  COTS is an acronym for 
Commercial, Off-The-Shelf.

But for scale - and a difference in quantity can make for a difference in 
quality - there are parts of a Military which are the same as those in a 
commercial enterprise: a widget is requested, a widget is bought, a widget 
is delivered.

There are other parts of a Military which have no civilian equivalent and 
for those a custom-made (Brit. 'bespoke') solution must be created.

What items/systems fall into which areas - is an inventory of deployed, 
battle-ready nuclear warheads 'just another inventory'? - is a matter of 
tradition, law, precedent, tomorrow's emergencies and tedious 
specification.

DD
```

```
"Rick Smith" <rs847925@gmail.com> wrote in message 
news:97f26e91-fd9c-431b-90a2-c034253056ce@googlegroups.com...
> On Wednesday, March 20, 2013 8:41:08 PM UTC-4, Pete Dashwood wrote:
>> Rick Smith wrote:
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> [snip]

<snip>

COTS is often seen as a cost cutting measure but it may not work out that 
way in practice.  Government often has unique requirements that  standard 
COTS software does not provide.  I once worked for the U.S. Senate Computer 
Center (SCC) on their in-house developed payroll system.  It handled things 
such as special requirements for Senate offices sharing employees,  hiring 
re-employeed annuitants, and variouus special allotments.  These types of 
processing functions are not usually of value to most companies so vendors 
of COTS have no incentive to provide them.  At the SCC they evaluated many 
payroll and personnel packages and eventually picked one to replace the 
in-house system.  Of course this required us to make modifications in order 
tomake the system provide the required functions. These were not one time 
modifications, as every time the vender released new versions and we 
upgraded, we had to make the modifications all over again.  Naturally this 
did not result in monetary savings.
```

```
Rick Smith wrote:
> On Thursday, March 21, 2013 2:10:39 PM UTC-4, Charles Hottel wrote:
> [snip]
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> States differ in the types of taxes they collect.

Usually, where different jurisdictions are involved, people consider it 
difficult/impossible. I managed the database design of a European package 
that had to go into 14 countries, each of which had their own legal 
requirements in their own languages.

Our team had a specialist from each country working with us and we started 
by identifying what was common across all, then identifying what was 
specific to each country. Technically, it was pretty easy to provide hooks 
for "locality dependent processing" and let them plug in solutions for their 
own specific requirements. Everyone agreed the core processing and everyone 
signed it off. (The French resisted fiercely and demanded to develop their 
own system, but were overruled by Corporate. After some sulking and attempts 
to sabotage the effort, they finally came on board and got a solution which 
they eventually agreed was adequate to their needs...)

To everyone's amazement it was nowhere near as hard as had been at first 
supposed.

Again, it comes down to the will (and the resources) to do something.

Pete.
```
