###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: IT's Record of Failure Is Worse Than You Think

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net>
- **Date:** 2013-03-19T14:29:42-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<2pednb8TLNrtM9XMnZ2dnUVZ_hydnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<f60b7e17-b33b-4103-9378-b8c2ef17753d@googlegroups.com> <aqkc9kFfmjjU1@mid.individual.net> <1b08fcf3-73d8-42e8-8afe-64992780dce3@googlegroups.com> <aqmffdFtic3U1@mid.individual.net>`

```

"Bill Gunshannon" <bill@server1.cs.uofs.edu> wrote in message 
news:aqmffdFtic3U1@mid.individual.net...
> In article <1b08fcf3-73d8-42e8-8afe-64992780dce3@googlegroups.com>,
> Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com> writes:
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>>

<snip>

>> These beg the question: Are there some systems, written in
>> COBOL, so large and intractable, that projects to 'modernize'
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> Scranton, Pennsylvania   |         #include <std.disclaimer.h>

I have been following this tread with some interest.  Many of you may recall 
that I worked at a federal agency that wanted to replace a large COBOL based 
system with one written in Java.  In the past I made it a point not to 
mention the specfic agency and some of the details, but now that I am 
retired I feel more freedom in giving more of the details.

I worked for about 19 years for the U.S. Customs Service which became the 
Bureau of Customs and Border Protection (CPB) within the Department of 
Homeland Security.  The COBOL based system was called the Automated 
Commercial System (ACS) and was developed in chunks and enhanced over more 
that 20 years.

IIRC around 1992 they decided to replace it with a multi-tiered architecture 
based on PC's, UNIX servers and mainframes.  I specfically remember an email 
going out before any design or programming had been done, which gave the 
system completion date as somtime in 1995.  I knew as soon as I read that 
email that it was never going to happen.  After a couple years and many 
millions of dollars they decided that the architecture they were proposing 
to use was too costly.  They simply could not afford the hardware.  I do not 
understand why they could not have determined this up front.  They did 
develop a small subsystem which allowed them to call the project a success. 
With sucesses like that you certainlt do not need any failures!

Somewhat after this the IRS started their modernization project and for a 
while the stories coming out spoke of success and great progress.  So my 
agency began again to try and replace the ACS sytem following the IRS model 
of using a consortium of contractors.  The new system was called the 
Advanced Computing Environment (ACE).

Since I worked only on ACS, my perspective of the new system lies outside 
the actual development of ACE, and much of it comes from information I 
obtained from my supervisors and co-workers who worked on providing the ACE 
developers with detailed specifications of ACS functions that ACE also had 
to provide.

Most of the top management in the IT department came from the user side of 
things.  They were Custom's agents and had little or no experience in 
managing IT and they probably had no concrete idea of what it takes to write 
a significant computer program or even a tiny one.  This meant that they 
relied very heavily on the contractors for advice.  That advice was COBOL is 
old (not modern) so the new system should be in a more modern language, 
Java.

The management did have experience with using ACS and they had many ideas 
for how the design of ACE should be different.  So ACE was not just to be a 
recoding of ACS in Java.  ACS used Datacomm/DB as a DBMS and it was 
expensive, so management wanted to go with either Oracle or DB2.  They also 
used SAP to provide many of the ACE capabilities.

Eventually there were over 700 contractors working on ACE.  The programmers 
were regarded as a pool of available resources.  They would do their coding 
and send it to the testing group at which point they were an available 
resource in the programmer pool.  If the testing group uncovered problems 
they would usually have to be fixed by someone other than the original 
programmer.  The original programmmer would already be working on a new 
assignment.  I believe that this gave the programmers very little incentive 
to worry about errors since they would not have to fix their own errors.

IIRC correctly they were spending $200 to $300 million a year on development 
of ACE.  This was a real money trough.  Over the years many federal 
employess retired and went to work for the ACE contractors.  I personally 
know one of them and his net salary as a contractor was greater than what 
his gross salary had been from the federal government.  Eventually the 
majority of the money appropriated was spent.  I don't remember the total 
amount that was spent. I remember a conference call where the IT deparment 
handed over the day to day mangement of ACE to another department within 
CBP.  They said that most of the money had been spent and they only got 
about 20% of what they had originally wanted.  About a month after this I 
had my annual review and I mentioned this to my supervisor.  He said that 
while he had not attended the meeting, he would be very surprise if they got 
more than 5% to 10% of what they wanted.

Of course when you spend this kind of money you call whatever you get a 
success because you do not want to look bad!

So now they have a partially deveoped ACE system which has replaced some 
functions of ACS but not all.  I was lucky as I worked on the Air Manifest 
System (AMS) which was considered one of the more complicated subsystems of 
ACS and as such it was left for later replacement in ACE and they ran out of 
money before they go to it.
```

Rick Smith wrote:
> On Saturday, March 16, 2013 7:07:31 PM UTC-4, Pete Dashwood wrote:
> [snip]
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> 'chunks'" can result in "large chunks".

Then you decompose the "large chunks" even further. In over 40 years of IT 
experience I have never seen a projected system that could not be decomposed 
in a sensible way. I have dealt with large corporations, small/medium 
businesses, and Government. It is about the will to do something, the 
imagination to overcome obstacles and the competence to make it happen.

I would agree that in the early days there was a marked lack of experience 
and the approaches we used were all we knew. But today there is no excuse 
for large scale IT project failure.

(Sadly, we have not yet managed to eradicate incompetence, and that is where 
most of the trouble stems from.)

>
> <
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
> to handle a fairly simple payroll for 1,300 people.'"

There is NO SUCH THING as a SIMPLE payroll!

However, there are some excellent packages which contain lifetimes of 
experience in this field.

The above, to me, smacks of politics rather than professionalism.

We have a similar situation in NZ at present where a hugely expensive 
Payroll package was purchased from Australia to pay teachers. The situation 
is so bad that you can be forgiven for thinking there must have been 
corruption involved. After some discussion with persons who should know, I'm 
satisfied there probably isn't (not at the higher levels anyway) but 
political pressure has figured large in making this a fiasco.

Incompetence can do what immorality does and make an even worse job of it...


>
> "California also has a history of large IT project failures,
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> "Sooner or later, COBOL will kill us all"

>
> "I read with some trepidation that the computer language
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I didn't bother responding to this, but I did enjoy the article.

It's pretty much what you might expect from a blind cyclist... :-)


> These beg the question: Are there some systems, written in
> COBOL, so large and intractable, that projects to 'modernize'
> them are doomed to failure?

NO EXISTING SYSTEM is "doomed to failure" if the will and the means to do it 
are available.

1. The system exists.

2. Therefore the system was able to be programmed. (It has rules which can 
be presented in computer sensible form.)
    Even if these rules have changed over time it is still possible to 
identify what the rules are. (If it weren't, the current system could not 
run).

3. If the system was able to be programmed, the system is able to be 
re-programmed.

4. It is therefore possible to rebuild ANY existing system. (QED)

There are a couple of riders to the above:

1. If you approach it from the point of view of re-doing it IN COBOL, it may 
well be more trouble than it is worth.
2. You may need to use a completely different approach from that employed 
originally.
3. You can only be guaranteed to rebuild the functionality which is 
CURRENTLY available. If you want improvements, it MAY be possible.
4. The amount of effort and resource needed to do a rebuild may be huge, it 
may not be justifiable, but if it HAS to be done, it CAN be done.

As someone who makes a living salvaging COBOL, I have yet to see a system 
that cannot be salvaged. New and better tools to do this are appearing all 
the time (I'm writing some of them... :-)) Even in the last 10 years new 
approaches to the problem have evolved. Data driven Migration is an approach 
that PRIMA has pioneered and it is working very well. Although data 
structures and types within an organization can change over time, the 
fundamental elements that are required by that process don't change much.. 
(A payroll will need a pay rate, and someone to be paid, for example...) The 
existing system (especially if it is written in COBOL) conveniently 
identifies the elements needed. (PICTURE clauses...) Once a data analysis 
has been done (PRIMA has a tool to do this automatically) and the data is 
available in a modern repository, it is then possible to use it for both Old 
and New development. (Again, we transform the existing codebase 
automatically and make it access through a generated Data Access Layer 
comprised of objects.) Legacy processes as it always has, but now it gets 
its  data from the common repository.

New processes are developed separately and may or may not be in COBOL. 
(Whatever it is written in, it will be written as objects) Both Old and New 
technology use the SAME data repository. The Legacy code continues to update 
it and the new systems use it in new ways. No Batch synchronizing, everybody 
is singing off the same hymn sheet. The repository is accessible (through 
the DAL objects) from the desktop or the network and it is accessible by Old 
and New program code and even by ad hoc scripting. (Note that there is NO 
embedded SQL in the transformed legacy or in the new development. Separation 
between Business Logic and Data Access is maintained.)

The main difference is that the New is object oriented and the Old is not.

Useful routines in the legacy code can be refactored for use by the new code 
or they can be rewritten in new code. (Depends on priorities and resources 
available)

In this way, the NEW code gradually supersedes the OLD. There is no pressure 
to migrate, there is no Big Bang Migration, just the evolution of the system 
towards New technology.

No matter how large or convoluted the legacy may be, it keeps running until 
it is replaced or overtaken by events.

This is a different idea from sitting down and trying to rebuild a system in 
COBOL.

Pete.
```

Rick Smith wrote:
> On Sunday, March 17, 2013 7:25:12 PM UTC-4, Pete Dashwood wrote:
>> Rick Smith wrote:
…[36 more quoted lines elided]…
> I apologize if I misunderstood what you wrote.

Thank you for your clear and reasoned comments, Rick. (So much better than 
just saying it is "absurd"...)

I don't think you have misunderstood, althugh I might have been clearer in 
places.

I'll address your "first, second and third, as follows:

First:  Yes, an important distinction. However, I think we can agree my 
"proof" refers to existing systems?

Second: The "current system" can certainly run (in the sense of execute 
code) but it can't "run" (in the sense of providing an ongoing service) if 
the rules cannot be identified, because it is impossible to know whether the 
results produced are "correct" or not.

Third: I don't think it "denies" the proof. Rather it says that even if you 
"accept" the "proof" as being reasonable, you STILL can't modernize your 
system without the will and the resources to do so. It is a logical 
conjunction with the syllogism of the "proof". Having the time and the money 
(and the will) does not make your modernization project viable in and of 
itself.

>>
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> [rest snipped]

Pete.

Bill Gunshannon wrote:

> on.

Bill, I have accepted many projects that were considered "impossible" and 
successfully concluded them. In fact, I gave a lot of thought as to why tech 
guys would even SAY something was "impossible". The conclusion I reached was 
that they didn't WANT to do it, or they didn't KNOW HOW to do it, or they 
did not have the IMAGINATION to get a solution from "outside the box", or 
(and this is something like .0001% of situations) it actually was 
impossible.

In general, when tech guys say: "That's impossible.", what they really mean 
is: "I don't know how to do it."

I had one particular case where IBM said it was impossible. That was 
supposed to end all argument. It was their hardware and software (System 370 
running OS/VS2) and the analyst concerned took the approach that they were 
the ultimate authority and if they said it couldn't be done, it couldn't be.

But the User WANTED it to be done. (It involved automatically refreshing 
3270 screens based on an event at any one of the workstations. As the 
terminals were in "Conversational Mode" this cannot be done; you must press 
Enter before the system can respond.) The User asked me if it could be done 
and I said:"I'll have a look at it..." A week later, we invited IBM to come 
and see 3270 terminals being refreshed automatically. They were a little 
petulant as they were on record as saying it was impossible... When they saw 
the demo there was some excited chattter in their huddle then they said:"To 
do this you must have used non-standard software and it invalidates our 
warranty. We will not support the system." My Boss looked at me anxiously, 
but I said: "There is no non-standard software. The solution was achieved 
with COBOL, MFS and imagination." After that I was legend in that place and 
was immediately given pretty much whatever I asked for... :-) The point is 
that "impossible", unless it can be shown clearly and uneqivocally to be so, 
is very often just "difficult".

I had a friend, sadly now gone to the big computer centre in the sky, who 
was irritated by my "can do"attitude... (He was Scots... :-)) "OK Pete, if 
nothing is impossible, fart in a bottle and rattle it... hang your arse out 
the window then go down the road and throw stones at it..." I can hear him 
to this day and it is a good reality check :-)

I would LOVE to take a crack at the US IRS but it is unlikely I'll get the 
chance... :-)

Hwever, some of the posts in this thread indicate that I am not the ONLY one 
who has hit on the idea of driving modernization through the data base. I 
believe if that approach is taken, it would be possible to evolve even the 
massive systems you allude to into a modern incarnation.

>
>>                                                       Although data
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> and the list goes on and on.

Yes, I know. I said so in an earlier post :-)
>
>>
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> gain in moving from the current system.

No, it gives you the OPTION to "stay with COBOL" or to move on to something 
else. Hopefully, the new web site I am currently preparing will explain this 
a bit better.
>
>>
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> objects.

Apart from abstract ideas, I think you'll find it actually is... :-)

> It is really funny.  OO proponents are fond of saying that the
> problem is
> preceduralists just can't undersand the OO paradigm.  Seems to me the
> opposite is also true.

there are people in bith camps for whom that is true.

As I am familiar and have worked with both paradigms over many years, your 
comment does not apply to me. I can tell you that OO is NOT a "buzzword" any 
more than ISAM or "Modular programming" are buzzwords. Object Orientation is 
a paradigm that is very well suited to networked systems. As there is a 
tendency for more and more systems to be moved to a networked solution, it 
makes sense to salvage existing COBOL by "objectizing" it. Our tools can 
refactor ALL of the indexed IO in COBOL and move it to a separate layer 
(DAL). The code base does not suddenly become "OO COBOL" in the true sense, 
but it becomes "object aware" and can now use the DAL objects and any other 
standard components (written in any other languages), which it certainly 
couldn't do previously.

Again, it turned out that if you wanted to do this badly enough (I did...) 
it was not impossible... However, it did take some years and it was NOT 
easy...
 :-)

>
>>                                                            Both Old
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> my DMS11 database?

Through a DAL object generated by the Toolset. (It is a Data Access LAYER).

It is the same principle as you have currently when you add a new 
description to the dictionary and the system generates DASDL for it. All of 
your current access is embedded in your COBOL similarly to if it was 
accessing ISAM, except it is DMS11. Our tools would find and translate this 
code into an object invoke.

We do not currently have a solution written for DMS11 as we focus on 
Relational Database Management Systems, but we'd certainly produce one if 
we were paid to :-)



>
>>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Change for the sake of change.  See comment above about paradigms.

No, it is change for the sake of improvement in a new environment.
>
>>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> financially feasable.  :-)

I'd like to know how you managed to compile this multi-million line 
monolith... :-) Most COBOL compilers I have worked with do not have 
UNLIMITED code and data limits.

Don't think for one minute it can't be broken into pieces... Does it contain 
out-of-line PERFORMS or CALLs...?

You're saying something is technically and financially infeasible because 
you think it is.

I'm not saying either way (because I haven't seen it) but my instinct and 
experience tells me that anything THAT big is unlikely ot be "perfect" and 
therefore can PROBABLY be improved.
>
>>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Nice thought, looks good on paper, doesn't fit every reality.

I don't claim "EVERY" reality, but we haven't found a case yet where we were 
unable to help. (Believe me, if I thought it wasn't viable, I wouldn't take 
it on and I would tell the client why... After some years of doing this I 
have developed confidence in our tools and, as I designed and wrote most of 
them, I understand what they can and cannot do. There ARE limitations but it 
doesn't really have to do with the SIZE of the system.

	Robert Wessel <robertwessel2@yahoo.com> writes:
> On Tue, 19 Mar 2013 16:18:49 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> Cobol is 999,999 lines.

OK, an exageration.  I will have to go ask on the Unisys group just what
the limits are, But estimates of the Unisys COBOL run by the IRS are in
the 50 million line range.  (Maybe it has lots of comments.  :-)

But without reposting all the other stuff you said, Pete, (Which I enjoy
reading) let me describe "the impossible task" as I know it from my time
as a governemtn contractor.

Create a replacement program in Java or C# for a 50 million line
COBOL/DMS11 program.  It has to be done in less than two years.
It has to run perfectly.  There can be no errors in its output.
There can be no bugs in the program.  it has to exactly replace
all of the functionality of the old program.  And lastly, you have
to estimate the cost 1 to 2 years before you begin the project and
you can not have any cost or time over-runs.  Oh yeah, and the
cost has to be acceptable by Capital Hill beancounters who don't
know how to use a computer beyond Microsoft Office.

Don't get me wrong.  I have done my share of impossible jobs.  The
last one was real fun.  Several years ago The University Print Shop
bought this whiz-bang Xerox Print Station.  They wanted to put it
on the network so departments could submit documents directly to it.
The University Data Center said. "Can't be done".  The Xerox Rep
repeatedly told them, "We do it all the time."  The University Data
Center said. "Can't be done".  Print Shop called me.  I set up a
Unix box that could take LPR jobs from client PC's from things like
MS Word or Excel process them and submit them to the Novel Netware
Queue the Xerox wanted.  Worked like a charm.  The University Data
Center said. "Can't be done".  And threatened to make them take it
off the campus network.  to the best of my knowledge, it is still
running today.

Most things are not impossible and very few technically impossible
but the are exceptions

bill
```

```
On Sunday, March 17, 2013 8:18:06 AM UTC-4, Rick Smith wrote:
[snip]

> These beg the question: Are there some systems, written in
> COBOL, so large and intractable, that projects to 'modernize'
> them are doomed to failure?

COBOL use at the Social Security Administration is discussed
in a report dated May 12, 2012, at,
< http://oig.ssa.gov/sites/default/files/audit/full/pdf/A-14-11-11132_0.pdf >

"SSA’s IT environment includes hundreds of applications and an array
 of technologies. While the Agency has increased its use of more
 modern programming languages in its applications, it relies on
 legacy applications programmed with Common Business Oriented
 Language (COBOL) to process its core workloads, such as retirement
 and disability claims. As of June 2010, SSA had over 60 million
 lines of COBOL code."


"DIVERGENT VIEWS OF COBOL

"In a 2007 report,[3] the National Research Council of the National
 Academies stated that newer programming languages offered greater
 capabilities than COBOL. The report also concluded that
 applications written in COBOL are more cumbersome to maintain.[4]
 Moreover, a 2009 Social Security Advisory Board (SSAB) report[5]
 stated that the computer industry generally viewed COBOL as
 obsolete, and the language lacked industry support. Additionally,
 Congress has expressed concern over SSA’s use of COBOL and the
 length of time the Agency requires to replace it.[6]

"On the other hand, other reports have indicated that COBOL remains
 in widespread use. A November 2008 Datamonitor white paper[7]
 reported that 75 percent of business data and 90 percent of
 financial transactions are processed in COBOL. Moreover, a
 Harris Interactive May 2009[8] survey found that the average
 American relies on COBOL at least 13 times a day for such
 activities as cellular telephone calls, credit card transactions,
 and travel reservations. Moreover, IT executives from six of the
 largest insurance and financial organizations stated COBOL remains
 in use in their industries. For more information on COBOL’s use in
 the public and private sectors, see Appendix B."

----
3 National Research Council of the National Academies, Social
 Security Administration Electronic Service Provision, A Strategic
 Assessment, August 2007.
4 Id. 
5 SSAB, Bridging the Gap: Improving SSA’s Public Service Through
 Technology, April 2009. 
6 Clearing the Disability Claims Backlogs: The Social Security
 Administration’s Progress and New Challenges Arising from the
 Recession: Hearing Before House Committee on Ways and Means
 (Subcommittee on Social Security), 111th Cong. 111-38 (2009)
 (statement by Congressman Sam Johnson). 
7 Datamonitor, COBOL – Continuing to Drive Value in the 21st
 Century, November 2008. 
8 The survey was commissioned by Micro Focus and conducted by
 Harris Interactive. 
-----


"SSA has adopted Java as a standard for new development projects.
 During the last decade, SSA has increased its use of Java to
 build new applications and modernize core COBOL applications
 with new interfaces, integration points, and business processes.
 Figure 1 shows that while 439 of SSA’s applications contain COBOL,
 186 applications contain Java (86 of those 186 applications
 include both COBOL and Java in the same application)."


"SSA does not perform straight conversions of COBOL application
 code to a more modern programming language."

"During our audit period, Forrester Consulting completed an
 SSA-commissioned assessment of the Agency’s use of COBOL.
 Forrester found that large organizations with workload
 characteristics similar to SSA had no plans to eliminate their
 use of COBOL. Furthermore, these organizations indicated that
 they have been able to enhance their use of COBOL systems by
 using Java in conjunction with them. However, Forrester
 recommended that SSA take an evolutionary approach to
 modernization by replacing COBOL applications that no longer
 meet its business needs. The report also recommended that the
 Agency use Java for modern workloads like Internet applications
 but continue using COBOL for its high-volume core processing of
 transactions until Java’s ability proves capable. It should be
 noted that SSA’s modernization approach is consistent with
 Forrester’s recommendations." [Footnotes removed]

My impression overall is that SSA is in no hurry to replace
COBOL, even if takes longer than the 10-15 years one consultant
estimated.

It is very interesting reading. Thanks for posting it.

For myself, I discount immediately anything commissioned by Micro Focus or 
Gartner because these are obviously vested interests and, unless they can 
quote solid data (and they invariably don't...), there is just no 
credibility in their claims. I'm not impressed by the "fact" that the 
average American interacts with COBOL 13 times a day; it is impossible to 
verify and even if it were true, so what? Just because there is a lot of 
COBOL lying about doesn't mean it shouldn't be replaced. If I said the 
average NZer has NO contact with COBOL during a day, but still manages to 
use Banks, Airlines, and Insurance companies, not to mention buying stocks 
and shares, what does it prove? We converted our COBOL quicker than the USA? 
Given that our population is about the size of London/Finchley, NY/Queens, 
or LA/Burbank, it would be sad if we hadn't...

Nevertheless, there are other parts of the above which contain a description 
which has a ring of truth about it and it seems likely to be accurate.

As to the 10-15 years, watch what happens as pressure for modernization 
increases... :-)

Pete.

up millions and millions of lines of COBOL IN INDUSTRY.  And we haven't

National Finance Center (NFC) 25 million lines.

PPT Presentation [for all the good the link address will do],

Legacy Payroll/Personnel System (PPS) implemented in 1983

– Based on mainframe technology (COBOL, IDMS/DC, and ADS/O programming)

– 25 million-plus lines of code resulting from continuously modifying
 complex source code to accommodate USDA and other agency-unique payroll
 and personnel resource requirements

– Approaching the end of its lifecycle
-----

Intended replacement is COTS with an estimated completion date of
FY2016 (30 September 2016). With some customers/features(?) phased
in earlier.

In addition to the above, the National Finance Center (NFC)
is replacing its PROP system with the Corporate Property

Of particular interest is this from a PPT presentation,
< https://docs.google.com/viewer?a=v&q=cache:w18yunj0-K8J:cod.nfc.usda.gov/FMT/2009FMT/BS-10%2520CPAIS.ppt+&hl=en&gl=us&pid=bl&srcid=ADGEESiy-_04xIvjSrDfhCihO9s5nHzoVgXCQ0JqBJpoBD7VJYkGdIip582B-YjqVME5BEje4hkelbA000pCwfobg7nBcRSJpKzOGJJKH-9r351eOislXi24-UHKkQzT_R-Qp9XNUOzy&sig=AHIEtbQuktTk5L-6yWc5Ol1u7iH5TSF7wA >

 Major concerns regarding PROP:

...
 The Program Code is COBOL (COmmon Business-Oriented Language)
 - COBOL programming language is condensed with each new version;
   verbs currently used in the PROP program are eliminated with
   each new upgrade.
 - PROP functionality is reduced with each new upgrade.
...
-----

Also, as of 2012, the Small Business Administration (SBA) has
decided to remain with COBOL for their loan systems, but with
rehosting from a mainframe to UNIX using a 'newer version' of
COBOL.

The pattern I see is: remain with COBOL for that which resembles
insurance and banking, PeopleSoft for payroll and personnel,
and SAP for other accounting functions. Though most 'modernization'
for the military is still undefined.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: IT's Record of Failure Is Worse Than You Think

```

I really liked this post, Bill. Fair comments reflecting your position with 
some nice analogies.

Comments below...( with some marked snippage of previous)

Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> In article <94e4a20d-9fa7-44ce-a0e5-c00e556ab261@googlegroups.com>,
> Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com> writes:
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
> much COBOL is really out there).

Personally, I don't care "how much COBOL is out there", what I care about is 
the functionality it provides and safeguarding that functionality into the 
future.

Let's just say, for example,  there are several TRILLION lines of COBOL 
currently being executed daily.

It is still a TRILLION less than there were 20 years ago.

Can you see how impossible it is to verify these claims?

Can you see that it doesn't matter anyway, if automated tools can be used to 
replace it? (And we are finding that they can be...)

Here is a true figure than CAN be verified:  850 COBOL programs written in 
COBOL '85 (say, 2.5 million LOC). Refactored AUTOMATICALLY to use objects 
which can be written in any language, and able to share their data with New 
Technology, in just under 2 weeks. To do this manually was estimated at 
around 2 man/years of effort.

Tools can make a major difference.


>
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> programs have got along just fine without them for decades.

I reckon that's a valid argument, but only to a certain limit.

You have to look at what the additional capabilities are, and whether they 
can add value to the existing functionality.  Things may have been "getting 
along just fine" because nobody dared to implement anything new or to touch 
the existing code.

The tail shouldn't wag the dog; if new or enhanced functionality is required 
(whether it is because of new technology and languages empowering it, or 
not...) then it is the responsibility of the IT department to provide it. 
Historically, they haven't, and it is the long delays in development which 
led people to "do their own thing" with spreadsheets and databases, almost 
as soon as those capabilities became available.

>
>>                            The report also concluded that
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> people to do COBOL.

No Bill, it isn't. I know from my own experience (and that of a number of 
other sites) that COBOL maintenance IS more cumbersome than modern component 
based approaches.

Maintaining countless lines of low level code (even in a high level language 
like COBOL) is much harder, more error prone,  and more time consuming than 
having functionality encapsulated to localised areas of the system which use 
standard Classes and are written in languages intended for programmers, 
rather than accountants.



> My last COBOL position (first half of last year)
> was just such an example.  6 month old SCR waiting because no one knew
> how to do it.

Whatever the reasons for it, can you imagine the effect this has on the 
perception of the people waiting for it?

> Even the desire to replace the COBOL (not because it
> didn't do the job but because they had no one to work with it) was
> stalled because in order to replace it you first have to understand
> it. None of these the fault of COBOL, but management and academia.

I don't think the decline of COBOL has been due to any innate fault in the 
Language, so I agree with you here.


>
>>  Moreover, a 2009 Social Security Advisory Board (SSAB) report[5]
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> automobile is obsolete because you can't drive to Mars in it.

Hmmm...if you need to go to Mars, then it is a valid point. I think it is 
more like saying: "Why use a horse and buggy, when I have a 4WD?"


> Anything that still does the job it was designed to do while the job
> still needs to be done is hardly obsolete.

Agreed. But that doesn't preclude an alternate way of doing the job, which 
may have benefits beyond the specific job in question. If a COBOL system 
does the job, but a new Object Oriented system also does it, and the new 
system has the advantage of producing Objects that can be re-used in other 
areas outside of the specific system, then, even though the COBOL system 
meets the requirement, it would be better if it were replaced. NOT to do so 
is just hanging on to an emotional attachment...

>
>>            and the language lacked industry support.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> of these that also use COBOL and have no plans to replace them.

I think we've been here before :-)

Plans change. They change over time, personnel changes, and political 
pressures. Because something may be true today, does not mean it will be 
true tomorrow.

COBOL DOES "lack industry support" because there is no need to support it. 
It has limited relevance to today's environment, except in certain places 
where they are still asleep in the Fortress. The Old Guard are dying off and 
as New Technology shows itself to be equal to the tasks, there will be more 
pressure on the Fortress until finally it must crumble. As noted elsewhere, 
it is just a matter of time...


>
>>                                                       Additionally,
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> moves whichever way the wind blows.  Andin a place full of blowhards.

LOL! Nice one... and probably fair too.
>
>>
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> See, it's not just me saying this.

The Harris Interactive  Report was commissioned by Micro Focus. ('Nuff 
said... Although they are a good company selling good products they seem to 
have lost touch with reality when it comes to COBOL.)

The "six largest insurance company" executives are correct that it remains 
in use, FOR NOW... Other "large insurance comapnies" (Outside the USA) have 
either moved already or are working on it. You should understand that USA is 
nowhere near the world leader when it comes to insurance 
(http://www.insurancenetworking.com/news/insurance_reinsurance_life_health_property_casualty_am_best-26826-1.html); 
you can bet they are watching what is happening in the companies that ARE 
the World leaders...

Here is something quite interesting about the effect on the COBOL market 
that the move to modernize is having: 
http://www.itnews.com.au/News/300815,allianz-taps-finance-it-overhauls-for-cobol-skills.aspx

"It has never been easier to find COBOL programmers."  Notice that one of 
the three top Australian insurers is moving to retain COBOL. I wonder how 
long that can last? They have a bad year or get creamed by their 
competitiors and it is a stick he can be beaten with...

The last project I managed in Auckland was for a NZ insurer who was very 
much driven by what their competitors were doing also. (Both companies had 
moved off COBOL years ago.) Watching the opposition seems especially endemic 
in the Insurance industry...

It is also interesting to note that the largest insurer in the world (Japan 
Post) is moving to the Cloud using Force.COM and Git. They are committed to 
fully Object Oriented solutions (mostly with Java). There is no mention of 
COBOL I could find.

My point is that Fortess COBOL exists largely in the USA and it is only a 
matter of time before these castles are stormed, undermined, or simply 
overrun by the Barbarians... :-)

<snip>
>> My impression overall is that SSA is in no hurry to replace
>> COBOL, even if takes longer than the 10-15 years one consultant
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> floor laughing we continued our conversation.

Is he still laughing? Is he still the head of the IT Department?

>
> Take anything coming out of academia with a very large grain of salt.
> They are not really interested in reality but rather in steering the
> bus.  And God only knows what their true destination is.

Without claiming to be God, I believe I can shed some insight into their 
possible destination:

1. Give students marketable skills that will allow them to recover the costs 
of their Education.
2. Stimulate interest in learning and Education for its own sake.
3. Ensure the necessary skills to service and maintain a modern society are 
kept alive and available.
4. Set students on a path of personal growth that will make them better 
citizens, possibly even better people.

(I did discuss this with a friend who is a Maths lecturer at our local 
Polytechnic...)

Pete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: IT's Record of Failure Is Worse Than You Think

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

- **References:** `<f60b7e17-b33b-4103-9378-b8c2ef17753d@googlegroups.com> <aqkc9kFfmjjU1@mid.individual.net> <1b08fcf3-73d8-42e8-8afe-64992780dce3@googlegroups.com> <94e4a20d-9fa7-44ce-a0e5-c00e556ab261@googlegroups.com> <ar5pb9FaptgU1@mid.individual.net>`

```

I really liked this post, Bill. Fair comments reflecting your position with
some nice analogies.

Comments below...( with some marked snippage of previous)

Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> In article <94e4a20d-9fa7-44ce-a0e5-c00e556ab261@googlegroups.com>,
> Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com> writes:
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
> much COBOL is really out there).

Personally, I don't care "how much COBOL is out there", what I care about is
the functionality it provides and safeguarding that functionality into the
future.

Let's just say, for example,  there are several TRILLION lines of COBOL
currently being executed daily.

It is still a TRILLION less than there were 20 years ago.

Can you see how impossible it is to verify these claims?

Can you see that it doesn't matter anyway, if automated tools can be used to
replace it? (And we are finding that they can be...)

Here is a true figure than CAN be verified:  850 COBOL programs written in
COBOL '85 (say, 2.5 million LOC). Refactored AUTOMATICALLY to use objects
which can be written in any language, and able to share their data with New
Technology, in just under 2 weeks. To do this manually was estimated at
around 2 man/years of effort.

Tools can make a major difference.


>
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> programs have got along just fine without them for decades.

I reckon that's a valid argument, but only to a certain limit.

You have to look at what the additional capabilities are, and whether they
can add value to the existing functionality.  Things may have been "getting
along just fine" because nobody dared to implement anything new or to touch
the existing code.

The tail shouldn't wag the dog; if new or enhanced functionality is required
(whether it is because of new technology and languages empowering it, or
not...) then it is the responsibility of the IT department to provide it.
Historically, they haven't, and it is the long delays in development which
led people to "do their own thing" with spreadsheets and databases, almost
as soon as those capabilities became available.

>
>>                            The report also concluded that
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> people to do COBOL.

No Bill, it isn't. I know from my own experience (and that of a number of
other sites) that COBOL maintenance IS more cumbersome than modern component
based approaches.

Maintaining countless lines of low level code (even in a high level language
like COBOL) is much harder, more error prone,  and more time consuming than
having functionality encapsulated to localised areas of the system which use
standard Classes and are written in languages intended for programmers,
rather than accountants.



> My last COBOL position (first half of last year)
> was just such an example.  6 month old SCR waiting because no one knew
> how to do it.

Whatever the reasons for it, can you imagine the effect this has on the
perception of the people waiting for it?

> Even the desire to replace the COBOL (not because it
> didn't do the job but because they had no one to work with it) was
> stalled because in order to replace it you first have to understand
> it. None of these the fault of COBOL, but management and academia.

I don't think the decline of COBOL has been due to any innate fault in the
Language, so I agree with you here.


>
>>  Moreover, a 2009 Social Security Advisory Board (SSAB) report[5]
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> automobile is obsolete because you can't drive to Mars in it.

Hmmm...if you need to go to Mars, then it is a valid point. I think it is
more like saying: "Why use a horse and buggy, when I have a 4WD?"


> Anything that still does the job it was designed to do while the job
> still needs to be done is hardly obsolete.

Agreed. But that doesn't preclude an alternate way of doing the job, which
may have benefits beyond the specific job in question. If a COBOL system
does the job, but a new Object Oriented system also does it, and the new
system has the advantage of producing Objects that can be re-used in other
areas outside of the specific system, then, even though the COBOL system
meets the requirement, it would be better if it were replaced. NOT to do so
is just hanging on to an emotional attachment...

>
>>            and the language lacked industry support.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> of these that also use COBOL and have no plans to replace them.

I think we've been here before :-)

Plans change. They change over time, personnel changes, and political
pressures. Because something may be true today, does not mean it will be
true tomorrow.

COBOL DOES "lack industry support" because there is no need to support it.
It has limited relevance to today's environment, except in certain places
where they are still asleep in the Fortress. The Old Guard are dying off and
as New Technology shows itself to be equal to the tasks, there will be more
pressure on the Fortress until finally it must crumble. As noted elsewhere,
it is just a matter of time...


>
>>                                                       Additionally,
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> moves whichever way the wind blows.  Andin a place full of blowhards.

LOL! Nice one... and probably fair too.
>
>>
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> See, it's not just me saying this.

The Harris Interactive  Report was commissioned by Micro Focus. ('Nuff
said... Although they are a good company selling good products they seem to
have lost touch with reality when it comes to COBOL.)

The "six largest insurance company" executives are correct that it remains
in use, FOR NOW... Other "large insurance comapnies" (Outside the USA) have
either moved already or are working on it. You should understand that USA is
nowhere near the world leader when it comes to insurance
(http://www.insurancenetworking.com/news/insurance_reinsurance_life_health_property_casualty_am_best-26826-1.html);
you can bet they are watching what is happening in the companies that ARE
the World leaders...

Here is something quite interesting about the effect on the COBOL market
that the move to modernize is having:
http://www.itnews.com.au/News/300815,allianz-taps-finance-it-overhauls-for-cobol-skills.aspx

"It has never been easier to find COBOL programmers."  Notice that one of
the three top Australian insurers is moving to retain COBOL. I wonder how
long that can last? They have a bad year or get creamed by their
competitiors and it is a stick he can be beaten with...

The last project I managed in Auckland was for a NZ insurer who was very
much driven by what their competitors were doing also. (Both companies had
moved off COBOL years ago.) Watching the opposition seems especially endemic
in the Insurance industry...

It is also interesting to note that the largest insurer in the world (Japan
Post) is moving to the Cloud using Force.COM and Git. They are committed to
fully Object Oriented solutions (mostly with Java). There is no mention of
COBOL I could find.

My point is that Fortess COBOL exists largely in the USA and it is only a
matter of time before these castles are stormed, undermined, or simply
overrun by the Barbarians... :-)

<snip>
>> My impression overall is that SSA is in no hurry to replace
>> COBOL, even if takes longer than the 10-15 years one consultant
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> floor laughing we continued our conversation.

## Thread: a couple of strange standards — 52 this month / 66 total

_Topics: [`COBOL standards (ANS, ISO, 74/85/2002/2014)`](topics.md#standards)_

### a couple of strange standards

- **From:** charles.leviton@gmail.com
- **Date:** 2013-03-25T13:16:31-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<dc16edcd-ab32-4ea8-bcfe-047f6cb769ac@googlegroups.com>`

```
Saw these 2 standards in IBM's supplied list of code review recommendations
"Avoid THRU phrases in PERFORM statements "
"Avoid PERFORM, except PERFORM section"

Am rather confused since from day one I have always coded my programs to have
PERFORM para-name THRU para-name-exit  (this is IBM Mainframe, btw).

Any basis to making these recommendations? Does anyone adhere to these?

Thanks!
```

PERFORM...THRU (presumably to make it easier to someday add more complex 
code to what might have been a simple paragraph initially.

Most such coding standards are simply standards for the sake of having 
standards in my experience...

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: a couple of strange standards

- **From:** Clark F Morris <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca>
- **Date:** 2013-03-25T22:09:56-03:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<n0t1l8p2k44c32hqshh6mgs77jgghn3ejt@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<dc16edcd-ab32-4ea8-bcfe-047f6cb769ac@googlegroups.com> <kiqbiv$tui$1@dont-email.me> <1873311d-abe8-487d-b732-559d34c70ef9@googlegroups.com>`

```
On Mon, 25 Mar 2013 13:56:01 -0700 (PDT), charles.leviton@gmail.com
wrote:

>On Monday, March 25, 2013 4:22:44 PM UTC-4, Kerry Liles wrote:
>
…[43 more quoted lines elided]…
>pic.dhe.ibm.com/infocenter/ratdevz/v8r5/index.jsp?topic=%2Fcom.ibm.rsar.analysis.codereview.cobol.doc%2Ftopics%2Fcac_anconfig_describerules.html

Some of those standards make sense while others do not.  While NEXT
SENTENCE definitely should be avoided, some of the PERFORM rules
interfere with optimization done by IBM ENTERPRISE COBOL for z series
computers.  I would review the rules with someone who understands
COBOL and the balance between program clarity and program efficiency
as well as what current compilers are doing.  It also looks like there
are contradictions in the rules.

Clark Morris
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: a couple of strange standards

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** Doug Miller <doug_at_milmac_dot_com@example.com>
- **Date:** 2013-03-26T02:35:30+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<XnsA18EE6263A6DDdougmilmaccom@78.46.70.116>`
- **References:** `<dc16edcd-ab32-4ea8-bcfe-047f6cb769ac@googlegroups.com> <kiqbiv$tui$1@dont-email.me> <1873311d-abe8-487d-b732-559d34c70ef9@googlegroups.com> <n0t1l8p2k44c32hqshh6mgs77jgghn3ejt@4ax.com>`

```
Clark F Morris <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote in 
news:n0t1l8p2k44c32hqshh6mgs77jgghn3ejt@4ax.com:

> Some of those standards make sense while others do not.  While NEXT
> SENTENCE definitely should be avoided, some of the PERFORM rules
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> are contradictions in the rules.

I have to take issue with a portion of this. IMHO there is no "balance between program 
clarity and program efficiency"; that should *always* tilt in favor of increased clarity except in 
the most egregious cases of gross inefficiency -- e.g. clarity be damned, there is *no* 
excuse for ever writing an O(n^2) sort when there are so many O(n log n) algorithms 
available.

There was a time when the machine's time was more valuable than the programmer's.

It hasn't been that way for at least thirty years.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: a couple of strange standards

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-03-26T16:14:04+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<arci40FqkmvU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<dc16edcd-ab32-4ea8-bcfe-047f6cb769ac@googlegroups.com> <kiqbiv$tui$1@dont-email.me> <1873311d-abe8-487d-b732-559d34c70ef9@googlegroups.com> <n0t1l8p2k44c32hqshh6mgs77jgghn3ejt@4ax.com> <XnsA18EE6263A6DDdougmilmaccom@78.46.70.116>`

```
Doug Miller wrote:
> Clark F Morris <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote in
> news:n0t1l8p2k44c32hqshh6mgs77jgghn3ejt@4ax.com:
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> It hasn't been that way for at least thirty years.

So you haven't recently tried buying time on a Cray system, or employed a 
Bangalore COBOL guy for $6 an hour... :-)

Generally, though, you are right.

In terms of modern programming we are reaching a point where neither clarity 
nor efficiency is the topmost criterion.

ALL that matters is that it works, and it is cheap.

If you are not concerned with the "internals" of the functions you use, you 
are not concerned about how clearly or efficiently they are coded. 
Obviously, if something took an inordinately long time to run you might look 
for an alternative, but generally, "efficiency" measured in nanoseconds, or 
even in milliseconds, doesn't make the slightest bit of difference.

If you are writing your own components then you can apply whatever 
priorities you like in each instance, but it is unlikely you would just 
select ONE aspect of programming and decide that that is the MOST important 
thing for everything you code. It will vary with the task.

Be careful before you say "never"or "*no excuse*"...

One very good reason to write a O(n^2) sort would be as a teaching example 
on what not to do... :-)

Pete.
```

Clark F Morris wrote:
> On Tue, 26 Mar 2013 16:14:04 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> And that attitude is why many say www stands for World Wide Wait.

Try moving to fibre. I no longer have problems waiting for the Internet (10 
MBS down, 9.7 MBS up, and more if I want it and pay for it). And when I did, 
it had nothing to do with the "efficiency" of a program.

You personally cannot tell the difference between 10 nanoseconds and 15, 
Clark. You couldn't see a 100 nanosecond delay... None of us could.

My "attitude" is one of Realism. I'm simply tired of hearing people banging 
on about something that isn't discernible any more.

What used to take milliseconds now takes microseconds and what used to take 
microseconds now takes nanoseconds. These are improvements of orders of 
magnitude.

And please don't try and push my argument into saying I don't care about 
efficiency where it IS discernible. I am as annoyed as the next man when 
something takes 20 minutes and it could take 5. I'm talking about 
programmers who spend hours trying to save a few milliseconds IN COBOL (and 
I said as much), because someone has decided that "efficiency" will be a 
major criterion for program development. If "efficiency" is your PRIME 
criterion, then code in Assembler.

These days all that matters is that it works, and it is cheap. If you don't 
believe me, try arguing for a more expensive (more efficient?) solution with 
your friendly local bean counters....

>As
> in most cases, need for efficiency depends on many things such as:
> response time requirement, frequency of use, number of concurrent uses
> on a single computing entity such as an IBM z series LPAR etc.

Oh Puhleeze... Don't lecture me about what factors determine efficiency, or 
when it is actually needed; I've taught courses on it, written papers on it 
and debated it with experts. When online interaction first became available 
(we ran screens in 16K in Foreground 1 with an OS that took 4K on a system 
360 with 32K) and we realized there was a need for better utilization, I was 
there. As hardware increased logarithmically in power, I was there too. 
Today, a normal smartphone has over 100 times the power of that System 360. 
I've had 40+ years of this... I WAS that soldier. And I've also worked in 
non-commercial environments where efficiency WAS really important. (We 
didn't use COBOL...)

>For
> something used once a week in a non-peak usage period, fairly gross
> inefficiency is tolerable.  For other things, those nano seconds add
> up.

In a commercial computing environment they absolutely do NOT! Your system is 
spending thousands of times more time getting and putting data than it is in 
processing it.

>The attitude that efficiency is not that relevant is why many of
> the things seem slow or sluggish on my Intel Quad core computer that
> may be faster than any of the mainframes I worked on before the year
> 2000 and definitely it has more memory.

Bollocks! :-) First off, I never said efficiency is not relevant (another 
thing I get tired of is people spinning what I actually said without really 
understanding it)
and second off, if your Quad system "seems slow or sluggish" that can be 
caused by factors totally unrelated to inefficient programming. (your 
perception... maybe your expectations are too high :-)... maybe it needs 
more IO power... maybe it needs better configuration... maybe the registry 
and file system are fragmented to Hell...who knows? I wouldn't mind betting 
it can be improved WITHOUT changing any program code that runs on it.

My point was, (and still is), that "efficiency" at the nano or microsecond 
level is really not such a high priority IF you are using a high level 
language. If you are writing an application that REALLY needs to squeeze the 
juice then using COBOL to write it is self defeating. (However, given that 
most commercial applications don't fall in that category, then COBOL has 
been fine.)


>>
>> If you are writing your own components then you can apply whatever
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>>
Yep, I still stand by that.

We are also disagreeing here because we are talking about two completely 
different schools of programming. I take Object Oriented, component based, 
design and development as read; you don't operate with that model. In my 
world things are small and there simply isn't room for them to be grossly 
"inefficient", so it is less of a consideration. I would agree there is more 
room for inefficiency in monolithic procedural programming where very large 
programs can be written, but I still don't think it warrants a lot of 
attention in this day and age.

If you want to improve efficiency, improve your IO.

Pete.

```
On Mar 28, 2:40 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:


> Yep, I still stand by that.

Yes, we do notice that you are seldom swayed by other's different
experiences.


> We are also disagreeing here because we are talking about two completely
> different schools of programming. I take Object Oriented, component based,
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> attention in this day and age.

Actually with OO there is an overhead of object creation and
destruction, for example garbage collection, that does not occur with
serial reuse which is generally the model used by COBOL. In particular
it is usual for performance sensitive systems to serially reuse
objects in preference to creating new ones because that would be
grossly inefficient.

Also 'things being small' implies that a complete system that has a
large functionality is made up of a large number of small things. One
large 'monolithic' program has the overhead of a single file system
search and a single load (with all the overheads of memory allocation)
while each of your many small parts will incur those overheads,
perhaps even many times if the garbage collection overhead throws them
out.

Also those multitude of small parts may contain functionality in the
objects that is not used by a particular program. This means that
stuff is loaded that is not used creating [small] inefficiencies that
may not occur in a single large program, because it only has in it the
functionality, all of it, that is required.

>  there simply isn't room for them to be grossly "inefficient"

That is absolute nonsense and is mere dogma. 'Grossly inefficient' can
be done at any scale.
```

Then you obviously have no clue how object creation works, and have

> > Also 'things being small' implies that a complete system that has a
> > large functionality is made up of a large number of small things. One
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Not if it is serially reusable, as you stated above.

Your response does not seem relevant. How do you ignore having to load
many small libraries/class files to build the functionally required ?
If there are a dozen different class libraries in use then there must
be a dozen file search and loads.


> > while each of your many small parts will incur those overheads,
> > perhaps even many times if the garbage collection overhead throws them
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Whether a system has one thread or several depends on how the program
is designed, not on the language used. Being COBOL does not prevent
the use of threading, or multiple instances of the program.

What determines whether it is sensible to use serial reusability and/
or threads is the data flow. The types of applications that COBOL is
traditional used for is to process a stream of data. Serial
reuasability is more efficient then creating a new object for each
item in the stream (as already indicated) because of the cost of
object creation and subsequent garbage collection.

> Objects can have several instances executing simultaneously and this can be
> managed dynamically depending on demand.

Programs can have several instances executing simultaneously and this
can be managed dynamically depending on demand. For example Apache
will manage several instances of a program, for example COBOL programs
that are written to use fast-cgi (or similar).


> > Also those multitude of small parts may contain functionality in the
> > objects that is not used by a particular program. This means that
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> needed.

Maybe in your world.

> >>  there simply isn't room for them to be grossly "inefficient"
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Well, I guess it can if you really work at it.

On 28 Mar, 16:28, "Pete Dashwood" <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
wrote:
> Richard wrote:
> > On Mar 28, 1:13 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
…[38 more quoted lines elided]…
> There is so much you don't know, I see no point in pursuing this.

Or perhaps it is actually irrelevant.


> Either we have a very different view of what "serially reusability" is (I

'serial reuse' is reusing an existing resource for the next task
rather than destroying it and recreating a new one. The resource may
be a program, such as with Apache or CICS, an object, such as is done
by high performance OO programs to avoid the overhead of creating new
objects and have garbage collection recover the memory, or, for
example, record buffers.

> was using it with IMS/DC and CICS on IBM mainframes in the 1970s and am
> confident of the understanding I was taught, by people who actually DID
> understand what a dequeue boundary is in conversational or
> pseudo-conversational processing) or you just genuinely don't know what
> you're talking about.

'dequeue boundary' may well refer to _any_ queue that has a boundary.
Many queues are circular in that when the end is reached the 'next'
cell is the start.

Your IBM CICS 'experts' may have been using the term in a specific way
that is only relevant to their specific systems. What relevance does
it have to your OO programming in C# on Windows ? It certainly has
none on any system that I have or do use.

> Either way, I have nothing to add.
>
> Pete.
> --
> "I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."

On 3/25/2013 8:09 PM, Clark F Morris wrote:
> (Snip)
>>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I noticed the contradictory rules too, but they appear to be choices in 
the tool.  So if your shop requires PERFORM THRU, you select that rule 
for your code reviews, and if your shop forbids it you select the 
contradictory rule.  That seems fair to me, even though my preference 
would be not use THRU or SECTION.
```

#### ↳ Re: a couple of strange standards

- **From:** Doug Miller <doug_at_milmac_dot_com@example.com>
- **Date:** 2013-03-25T21:04:08+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<XnsA18EADFAE2C66dougmilmaccom@78.46.70.116>`
- **References:** `<dc16edcd-ab32-4ea8-bcfe-047f6cb769ac@googlegroups.com>`

```
charles.leviton@gmail.com wrote in news:dc16edcd-ab32-4ea8-bcfe-
047f6cb769ac@googlegroups.com:

> Saw these 2 standards in IBM's supplied list of code review recommendations
> "Avoid THRU phrases in PERFORM statements "
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> Any basis to making these recommendations? Does anyone adhere to these?

<dons Nomex suit>

IMHO, the first is excellent practice, the second insane.

I think PERFORM ... THRU is *always* a bad idea, and any perceived "need" for it simply a 
reflection of poor design: the only reason for ever using PERFORM <para-name> THRU 
<para-name-exit> is to enable jumping to the exit with a GO TO -- and if you think you need 
to do that, you have a poor design.

PERFORM ... THRU in any other context is a terrible practice: all it takes is one paragraph 
inserted -- or moved -- to radically change the program's behavior.

PERFORM <section> *instead* of PERFORM <paragraph> is crazy. There's no need for 
sections in the Procedure Division to begin with. There *used* to be, back when a big 
machine was one with 256K of core memory, but those days are long gone. Using sections 
*now* is a very bad practice, for the same reason that PERFORM ... THRU is a very bad 
practice: it makes it far too easy to screw up -- and there is *no* benefit from using either 
one.

Now I'm going to get some popcorn and watch the argument this is sure to start...
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: a couple of strange standards

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-03-26T15:58:04+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<arch5uFqf6mU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<dc16edcd-ab32-4ea8-bcfe-047f6cb769ac@googlegroups.com> <XnsA18EADFAE2C66dougmilmaccom@78.46.70.116> <efs1l8p6t958ui3rqcj0sijeav402886nn@4ax.com>`

```
Graham Hobbs wrote:
> On Mon, 25 Mar 2013 21:04:08 +0000 (UTC), Doug Miller
> <doug_at_milmac_dot_com@example.com> wrote:
…[43 more quoted lines elided]…
> Graham Hobbs

I disagree with both of you... :-)

When I first coded COBOL, in 1967, using the COBOL 61 compiler,  and a 
little later the same year using XEKB on ICL 1900s, I was taught to use 
PERFORM ..THRU.

The idea was that it clearly delineated the end of the PERFORM range. (It 
was code oriented rather than worrying about functionality)

As time went by my exerience expanded and I encountered "new" (to me, at 
least...) ways of coding COBOL.

In particular, I worked in one shop where the standard was PERFORM... 
SECTION.  This forced clear delineations of functionality rather than code. 
If you wanted to see how penal rates were calculated, there was a section 
for that, for example. Another section might deal with a certain type of 
customer and so on. I came to see that both constructs were useful and did 
their job. I never saw any "superioity" of one over the other, but I can say 
that today I code sections in COBOL.

Nowadays, on the occasions when I have to deal with COBOL, I am writing OO 
COBOL and it makes sense to delineate a Method by giving it its own section 
of code.

Since the advent of inline PERFORMs I have been using those too, but that 
requires neither section nor thru.

BEFORE the advent of inline PERFORMs I used a technique I picked up from 
programming NCR 315s - PERFORM  PARA THRU <some dummy para name> 
[VARYING... ] etc. where "some dummy para name" was an EXIT... (yeah, I 
know... this was from the days when Virtual Storage wasn't invented yet and 
everything was in real memory...) The SAME dummy para name was used as a 
common exit from ALL the PERFORMS and you would simply GO TO <dummy para 
name> when you had enough. It performed the same function as EXIT PERFORM 
does now but it was much uglier.

I tried it on a CDC Cyber 73 and it looped indefinitely. That kind of cured 
me of that.

The modern inline PERFORM is much more "elegant". (And so is EXIT 
PERFORM...)

I'm not really impressed by arguments about helping the compiler to optimize 
because it could only help with virtual paging and that is no longer 
something that programmmers even need to consider. (If you DO need to 
consider it, why are you writing your solution in COBOL?)

There are sound arguments both for and against using THRU or SECTIONs.

The fact is that it really doesn't matter; personal programmer preference 
(based usually on what you have been taught or whatever "authority" you want 
to support will make the choice for you, always assuming the local standards 
don't.

Pete.
```

>

…[3 more quoted lines elided]…

***************************************************
*
* NEW FUNCTIONALITY BEGINS HERE
*
***************************************************

> If you wanted to see how penal rates were calculated, there was a section 
> for that, for example. Another section might deal with a certain type of 
> customer and so on. I came to see that both constructs were useful and did 
> their job. I never saw any "superioity" of one over the other, but I can say 
> that today I code sections in COBOL.

Use of SECTIONs confers no benefit whatsoever that cannot also be achieved with 
paragraphs and comments -- but adds substantial danger to maintenance.
> 
> Nowadays, on the occasions when I have to deal with COBOL, I am writing OO 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> requires neither section nor thru.

And is a *far* better and wiser practice than either.
> 
> BEFORE the advent of inline PERFORMs I used a technique I picked up from 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> does now but it was much uglier.

Like I said... if you think you need to do that, you have a poor design.
> 
> I tried it on a CDC Cyber 73 and it looped indefinitely. That kind of cured 
> me of that.

Like I said...
> 
> The modern inline PERFORM is much more "elegant". (And so is EXIT 
> PERFORM...)

No argument from me on that score.
> 
> I'm not really impressed by arguments about helping the compiler to optimize 
> because it could only help with virtual paging and that is no longer 
> something that programmmers even need to consider. (If you DO need to 
> consider it, why are you writing your solution in COBOL?)

Or there.
> 
> There are sound arguments both for and against using THRU or SECTIONs.

Obviously I disagree. There are sound arguments against doing so. And there are 
arguments for doing so -- but not sound ones.
> 
> The fact is that it really doesn't matter; personal programmer preference 
> (based usually on what you have been taught or whatever "authority" you want 
> to support will make the choice for you, always assuming the local standards 
> don't.

That's not true.

The fact is, it *does* matter. It's not just personal preference. PERFORM ... THRU and 
PERFORM <section> both make maintenance more difficult and error-prone by making it 
possible to introduce errors in a program by the mere addition of a new paragraph (or by 
moving an existing paragraph). This danger simply _does_not_exist_ if those two wretched 
practices are avoided.

From http://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pont_Neuf :

--begin quoted text:

Le pont Neuf est, malgre' son nom, le plus ancien pont existant de Paris.

--end quoted text

Lit. 'He bridge Neuf is, in spite of his name, he more former bridge 
existing of Paris'.

Colloq. 'The pont Neuf is, despite its name, the oldest existing bridge in 
Paris'.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: a couple of strange standards

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-03-28T03:34:55+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<argechFmdo1U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<dc16edcd-ab32-4ea8-bcfe-047f6cb769ac@googlegroups.com> <XnsA18EADFAE2C66dougmilmaccom@78.46.70.116> <efs1l8p6t958ui3rqcj0sijeav402886nn@4ax.com> <arch5uFqf6mU1@mid.individual.net> <XnsA18F6238B78F8dougmilmaccom@78.46.70.116>`

```
Doug Miller wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in
> news:arch5uFqf6mU1 @mid.individual.net:
…[77 more quoted lines elided]…
>

In no way is that a "better way"... have you never maintained code where the 
code was changed but the comments weren't?

>> If you wanted to see how penal rates were calculated, there was a
>> section for that, for example. Another section might deal with a
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> to maintenance.

The use of SECTION in OO programs provides a convenient "logical" Method 
encapsulation, although I wouldn't argue you HAVE to do it that way.

As for "substantial danger to maintenance".... ANY time you change program 
code there is a risk, no matter whether it has SECTIONS or not. A good 
programmer contains that risk and does so irrespective of how the program is 
coded. Looking back over millions of lines of COBOL written in every 
conceivable style, across a good number of different platforms, I am 
persuaded that whether it was SECTIONed or not never had any bearing on 
getting it right.

>>
>> Nowadays, on the occasions when I have to deal with COBOL, I am
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
>>
Design???!!! I'm talking about a time when we simply WROTE code... flow 
charting was an "advanced technique" which was for nancy boys and sissies; 
REAL programmers wrote code.

>> I tried it on a CDC Cyber 73 and it looped indefinitely. That kind
>> of cured me of that.
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> there are arguments for doing so -- but not sound ones.

Fortunately, I no longer care enough to debate this in depth so I'll agree 
to disagree.

>>
>> The fact is that it really doesn't matter; personal programmer
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> The fact is, it *does* matter.

Not to me.

(And as I can only post based on personal experience my statement above is 
also true... for me. I have encountered a number of COBOL programmers for 
whom it was also true. Not for you? OK, I don't think you are wrong. I 
already said it is a matter of opinion...)

> It's not just personal preference.

No, it isn't if there are local standards in place. But I already covered 
that.

> PERFORM ... THRU and PERFORM <section> both make maintenance more
> difficult and error-prone by making it possible to introduce errors
> in a program by the mere addition of a new paragraph (or by moving an
> existing paragraph).

And yet, I have never encountered or made such an error. The fact that 
something makes something POSSIBLE just means extra care is required. But if 
you are doing a proper job you would be exercising proper care anyway. (Not 
to mention proper debugging and testing as well...) I don't agree that 
having this possibility makes maintenance "more difficult" because it never 
has in my experience. If maintenance is "error-prone" then the degree of 
care being exercised needs to be looked at again. It ISN'T error prone when 
I do it. :-)

> This danger simply _does_not_exist_ if those two
> wretched practices are avoided.

OK. But some of us aren't bothered either way. If someone says: "You're a 
COBOL programmer, can you fix this for us...?"  I don't know anyone who 
would look at it and say: "Ooooh... I see some SECTIONS in there... looks 
decidedly dodgy and dangerous... I think it's a bit too high risk for me... 
sorry, I'll have to  pass..."

This will be my final post on this because I really don't care enough to 
debate it in depth.

I'll read any reponses you make but won't respond.

Cheers,

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: a couple of strange standards

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2013-03-27T09:37:09-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<530dce66-50c7-49cf-b12c-d240e89824c4@googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<XnsA19077BDA1Adougmilmaccom@78.46.70.116>`
- **References:** `<dc16edcd-ab32-4ea8-bcfe-047f6cb769ac@googlegroups.com> <XnsA18EADFAE2C66dougmilmaccom@78.46.70.116> <efs1l8p6t958ui3rqcj0sijeav402886nn@4ax.com> <arch5uFqf6mU1@mid.individual.net> <XnsA18F6238B78F8dougmilmaccom@78.46.70.116> <argechFmdo1U1@mid.individual.net> <XnsA19077BDA1Adougmilmaccom@78.46.70.116>`

```
On Wednesday, March 27, 2013 11:44:31 AM UTC-4, Doug Miller wrote:
[snip]

> Perhaps it's time to re-examine your a priori biases. I've seen
> enough cases in which a SECTIONed program had plenty of bearing
> on getting it *wrong* to have convinced me that the use of
> SECTIONs in the Procedure Division is a dangerous practice -- one
> that has had no justification at all for more than thirty years.

Thirty years ago was 1983. Before the 1985 standard, one used
sections for sort input and output procedures, segmentation, and
declaratives. With the 1985 standard, sections for sort input and
output procedures were no longer required, segmentation was made
obsolete though still valid, and sections were still required for
declaratives. For the 2002 standard, sections are still required
for declaratives.
```

- **In-Reply-To:** `<arhf00Fto0qU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<dc16edcd-ab32-4ea8-bcfe-047f6cb769ac@googlegroups.com> <XnsA18EADFAE2C66dougmilmaccom@78.46.70.116> <efs1l8p6t958ui3rqcj0sijeav402886nn@4ax.com> <arch5uFqf6mU1@mid.individual.net> <XnsA18F6238B78F8dougmilmaccom@78.46.70.116> <argechFmdo1U1@mid.individual.net> <XnsA19077BDA1Adougmilmaccom@78.46.70.116> <530dce66-50c7-49cf-b12c-d240e89824c4@googlegroups.com> <arhf00Fto0qU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Wednesday, March 27, 2013 7:51:26 PM UTC-4, Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Rick Smith wrote:
[snip]

> > [...] For the 2002 standard, sections are still required
> > for declaratives.
[snip]

> So 2002 standard doesn't see sectioning as being prohibitively dangerous, in 
> some cases... :-)?

In fact, the 2002 standard makes it slightly easier to write
conforming code when using sections. The 2002 standard does not
require a paragraph name following the section header; the 1985
standard did.

----- 1985 conforming
       section-name-1 SECTION.
       paragraph-name-1.
           sentence.

----- 2002 conforming
       section-name-1 SECTION.
           sentence.

Thus, with 2002, it is possible to write a conforming COBOL
procedure division without even one paragraph name. <g>
```

```
On Thursday, March 28, 2013 5:59:17 PM UTC-4, Clark F Morris wrote:
> On Thu, 28 Mar 2013 18:36:01 +0000, Mike Fleming
> <{mike}@tauzero.co.uk> wrote:
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> Ever hear of PERFORM paragraph-name?

There is a recommendation in some Micro Focus documents to
PERFORM sections and restrict GO TO statements to paragraphs
within their same section. As a Micro Focus user, Mike's
statement is consistent with that recommendation.

Within those guidelines, one knows that a paragraph-name
will only be reached locally with a GO TO statement and the
absence of any paragraph-name means there are no GO TO
statements.

I, for one, would choose to not argue that Micro Focus is
mistaken about how to use its products to write effective
and efficient COBOL programs. <g>
```

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-03-29T12:12:28-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<f20aedb3-a48d-4678-8567-348e93903b52@oz4g2000pbc.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<XnsA18EADFAE2C66dougmilmaccom@78.46.70.116> <efs1l8p6t958ui3rqcj0sijeav402886nn@4ax.com> <arch5uFqf6mU1@mid.individual.net> <XnsA18F6238B78F8dougmilmaccom@78.46.70.116> <argechFmdo1U1@mid.individual.net> <XnsA19077BDA1Adougmilmaccom@78.46.70.116> <530dce66-50c7-49cf-b12c-d240e89824c4@googlegroups.com> <arhf00Fto0qU1@mid.individual.net> <89513562-4f5b-4b37-b66e-20dde6a6352f@googlegroups.com> <ib39l8legg81taqs508mqbpj30e9kqv2ir@4ax.com> <58f9l852unpftfl6f4f78vq845p6sd2qnf@4ax.com> <4a0ca2ab-0b71-4167-a0ca-c8fee64eb13b@googlegroups.com>`

```
On Mar 30, 6:46 am, Rick Smith <rs847...@gmail.com> wrote:
> On Thursday, March 28, 2013 5:59:17 PM UTC-4, Clark F Morris wrote:
> > On Thu, 28 Mar 2013 18:36:01 +0000, Mike Fleming
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
> statements.

Unfortunately there is no easy way to "know" that. It would be
possible to write an analysis program that could verify that all the
GO TOs are to paragraphs within the appropriate section*. It is also
possible that program could verify that all the PERFORMs are of
section headers. But without that you cannot 'know' that it is
correctly done.

De-nesting of deep logic also becomes more of a problem, and is less
likely to be done, especially where sections are grouped by logic
depth. Sections with GO TOs tend to become larger and breaking this
down into smaller units is less easy than with paragraph-only. It is
necessary to take the indented code outside the section and ensure
that it is a section in itself, this could reduce locality of code.


> one knows that a paragraph-name
> will only be reached locally with a GO TO statement

No, that is not necessarily true. It may be reached with a drop-
through, and usually is. The GO TO may be from above, to skip code
just above the paragraph label, or from below, to create a loop. These
create logic paths that are remote from the label itself and must be
determined by examination of the whole section before the paths
through that paragraph label are completely understood.


* I have found code where there was a check for an exception condition
that had, apparently, been copied from the section above and the GOTO
had not been changed to the correct -EXIT. This caused the exception
to process through the section above (which changed data so the
exception didn't get to the go to again) and drop-trough until it got
to the end of the section being performed. I have also found code
where a SECTION header was missing, the result was that code was
executed, wrongly, whenever the section above was performed - 'EXIT's
don't exit. If you assume that you "know" that every perform is of a
section then you may not check this is true.
```
